# Changelog

All notable changes to Celerity are documented here. This project follows [Keep a Changelog](https://keepachangelog.com/en/1.1.0/) and uses [Semantic Versioning](https://semver.org/spec/v2.0.0.html). Version numbers are produced by MinVer from the `v`-prefixed git tags.

## [Unreleased]

### Changed

- **The release pipeline now ships symbol packages, SourceLink, and deterministic builds** ([#190](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/190)), completing the milestone 2.0.0 multi-package CI work (the per-package metadata + shared-MinVer half had already shipped with the [#186](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/186)–[#188](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/188) split). The shared publishing settings now live once in [`src/Directory.Build.props`](src/Directory.Build.props) so all three packages stay in lockstep — they apply to every project under `src/` but only the three `IsPackable=true` packages act on them (the test / benchmark / fuzz / AOT-smoke projects set `IsPackable=false`). `release.yml` and `nightly-preview.yml` now pack with `-p:ContinuousIntegrationBuild=true`, upload **both** `*.nupkg` and `*.snupkg` as the build artifact, and the GitHub release attaches the `.snupkg`s alongside the `.nupkg`s; the existing `dotnet nuget push *.nupkg` loop is unchanged because it already auto-pushes each adjacent `.snupkg` to the NuGet.org symbol server (a clarifying comment now records that, so the snupkgs are not double-pushed).

- **All three packages now multi-target `net8.0`, `net9.0`, and `net10.0`** ([#189](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/189)), the multi-target half of the milestone 2.0.0 infrastructure work. `Celerity.Primitives`, `Celerity.Hashing`, and `Celerity.Collections` were single-target `net8.0`; they now build a TFM-matched assembly for each of the three frameworks, so `dotnet pack` emits `lib/net8.0`, `lib/net9.0`, and `lib/net10.0` in every `.nupkg` (with the same per-TFM transitive dependency graph: Collections → Hashing → Primitives) and NuGet restores the assembly compiled against the consumer's own runtime. **Decision recorded for the issue's "include net10.0 now or defer" question: included now** — net10.0 is GA (Nov 2025), LTS, and the SDK the family already builds with, so folding it in is free and gives net10 consumers a matched assembly and a home for future runtime-gated paths; **net9.0 is kept as required even though it is STS** (its support window is short) so 9.x consumers are covered during the transition; **net8.0 (LTS) remains the floor**, so the library still runs anywhere from .NET 8 upward. The shared TFM list lives once in [`src/Directory.Build.props`](src/Directory.Build.props) (`CelerityTargetFrameworks`), consumed by the three packages, the test project, and the AOT smoke test, so a future bump is a one-line change in lockstep. There are **no `#if`-gated code paths today** — the source compiles identically on every TFM (multi-targeting is the enabling step for later AVX-512 / JIT-gated optimizations, per the milestone vision); [`CONTRIBUTING.md`](CONTRIBUTING.md) documents the `#if NET9_0_OR_GREATER` / `NET10_0_OR_GREATER` convention plus the net8.0-fallback rule for when such a path is added. The trim/AOT analyzers and the CS1591 doc-coverage gate run on every TFM. Dev-only, non-shipped projects (`Celerity.Benchmarks`, `Celerity.Fuzz`) deliberately stay single-target net8.0 — they are tools, not packages.
- **CI now exercises every shipped TFM** ([#189](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/189)). The `build-and-test` job provisions the 8.0/9.0/10.0 SDKs, so a single `dotnet test` over the multi-targeted test project runs the full suite (3,114 tests) once per TFM on its own runtime, across the existing ubuntu/windows/macos matrix; the `aot-publish` job is now a `tfm ∈ {net8.0, net9.0, net10.0}` matrix that Native-AOT-publishes and runs the smoke-test binary for each framework. The `release.yml` / `nightly-preview.yml` pack jobs provision the three SDKs so `dotnet pack` can build all TFMs; `coverage.yml` is pinned to net8.0 (the source is identical across TFMs, so one TFM fully covers the line/branch surface and keeps the merged report deterministic without provisioning extra SDKs). The `benchmarks*.yml` / `fuzz.yml` workflows are unchanged — they build only their own single-target net8.0 projects.
- **Multi-package restructure — the monolithic library now ships as three NuGet packages** ([#186](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/186), [#187](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/187), [#188](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/188)), the defining work of milestone 2.0.0. The single `Celerity.Collections` assembly that contained every type is split into a three-project dependency layer mirroring the .NET package structure: **`Celerity.Primitives`** (lowest — `FastUtils`, the struct PRNGs, `VarInt`, `FastGuid`, `IRandomSource`) → **`Celerity.Hashing`** (`IHashProvider<T>`, every built-in hasher, `DefaultHasher<T>`, `HashQualityEvaluator`, `ProbeStatisticsEvaluator`, references Primitives for `FastUtils.NextPowerOfTwo`) → **`Celerity.Collections`** (the dictionaries, sets, frozen/perfect-hash collections, and streaming sketches, references both). The project layout under `src/` now has dedicated `Celerity.Primitives/` and `Celerity.Hashing/` projects; the existing `Celerity` project (assembly `Celerity.dll`, `PackageId` `Celerity.Collections`) keeps the collections. This is a **packaging breaking change**, not (with one exception below) a source one — all type namespaces are unchanged (`Celerity.Hashing.*`, `Celerity.Primitives.*`).
- **`FastUtils` moved from the root `Celerity` namespace to `Celerity.Primitives`** ([#187](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/187)), unifying it with the other primitives (`VarInt`, `FastGuid`, the PRNGs) that already adopted that namespace ahead of the split. This is the one **source-breaking** change in the restructure: callers of `FastUtils.FastMod` / `FastDiv` / `CountDigits` / `Log10` / `NextPowerOfTwo` add `using Celerity.Primitives;` (or qualify as `Celerity.Primitives.FastUtils`). Captured in [`docs/migration.md`](docs/migration.md#200--the-package-split).

### Added

- **`.snupkg` symbol packages, SourceLink, and a publish-time package-validation gate** ([#190](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/190)). Every shipped package now produces a separate **symbol package** (`IncludeSymbols` + `SymbolPackageFormat=snupkg`, portable PDBs), so `dotnet pack` emits `Celerity.Collections.snupkg` / `Celerity.Hashing.snupkg` / `Celerity.Primitives.snupkg` next to the `.nupkg`s and consumers can step into the library from a NuGet.org symbol-server debug session. **SourceLink** is enabled via the .NET 8+ SDK's built-in GitHub provider (no explicit `Microsoft.SourceLink.GitHub` `PackageReference` — the SDK auto-detects the remote) plus `PublishRepositoryUrl` / `EmbedUntrackedSources`, so each `.nupkg`'s `.nuspec` now carries a `<repository url="…" commit="<sha>">` stamp and each PDB maps every source file to `https://raw.githubusercontent.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/<commit>/…` (verified: a packed PDB resolves to the exact commit SHA). Builds are **deterministic** — `ContinuousIntegrationBuild=true` (set automatically in CI via the `CI` / `GITHUB_ACTIONS` env vars, and passed explicitly on the release/preview pack steps) normalizes the embedded source paths so the output is byte-reproducible, while local builds keep absolute paths for a good F5 experience. A new publish gate, [`.github/scripts/validate-packages.ps1`](.github/scripts/validate-packages.ps1), runs in `release.yml` / `nightly-preview.yml` right after pack and **fails the release** unless exactly the three expected packages were produced (catching a dev project that lost `IsPackable=false`, or a missing one), each has a matching `.snupkg`, and each `.nuspec` carries the required `license` / `readme` / `icon` / `projectUrl` and a `<repository>` with both a `url` and a `commit` (the SourceLink stamp) — with the declared README and icon actually embedded in the package. This satisfies the issue's acceptance criteria (a tagged release produces three `.nupkg` + symbols and validation passes for license / README / repo URL on each).

- **`Celerity.Hashing` and `Celerity.Primitives` NuGet packages** — each with its own `PackageId`, description, tags, icon, per-package `README.md`, and MinVer versioning shared in lockstep with `Celerity.Collections` (one `v`-tag drives all three, so they always release at the same version). `Celerity.Primitives` has no package dependencies; `Celerity.Hashing` depends on `Celerity.Primitives`; `Celerity.Collections` depends on both. `dotnet pack` on the solution now produces three `.nupkg`s with the correct transitive dependency graph. (Part of [#186](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/186) / [#187](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/187); the per-package metadata also covers the metadata half of [#190](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/190).)
- **Back-compat for existing `Celerity.Collections` consumers** ([#188](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/188)) on two axes. *Source:* the `Celerity.Collections` package declares non-private NuGet dependencies on `Celerity.Hashing` and `Celerity.Primitives`, so a project that references **only** `Celerity.Collections` transitively restores all three and keeps compiling against the hashers with no new `using`s or package references (the meta-package behaviour). *Binary:* `src/Celerity/TypeForwarders.cs` adds an `[assembly: TypeForwardedTo]` for every public type that moved out of `Celerity.dll` (44 hashing types, 11 primitives types), so already-compiled assemblies that bound those types in the old single `Celerity.dll` keep resolving them from their new home without recompilation. The only type not covered for binary identity is `FastUtils`, whose namespace deliberately changed (see above).
- `Celerity.Primitives/README.md` and `Celerity.Hashing/README.md` — concise per-package landing pages (what ships, the BCL-gap framing, links to the API reference), surfaced as the NuGet package readme.
- `PackageSplitTests` (`Celerity.Tests/Packaging`) — guards the split's assembly boundaries: asserts the collections live in `Celerity`, every hasher and the evaluators in `Celerity.Hashing`, and `FastUtils` + the PRNGs + `VarInt` + `FastGuid` in `Celerity.Primitives`; that the dependency direction is acyclic (Primitives references neither Hashing nor Collections; Hashing does not reference Collections); and that all three resolve and interoperate through a single `Celerity.Collections` project reference (the back-compat contract the test project itself relies on).
- `SimdReductions` in the `Celerity.Primitives` namespace — **fused / specialized SIMD reductions** that fill the gaps `System.Numerics.Tensors.TensorPrimitives` does *not* cover, the outcome of the milestone 2.1.0 research spike ([#197](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/197)). `TensorPrimitives` is already generic over `INumber<T>` and SIMD/AVX-512 accelerated for `Sum` / `Min` / `Max` / `Dot` / `IndexOfMax`, so this ships **only** the candidates that beat the BCL composition on a documented workload (the guiding rule), and **does not** reimplement those. (1) `MinMax(ReadOnlySpan<int|long|uint|ulong>)` returns both extrema as a `(Min, Max)` tuple in a **single pass**: composing `TensorPrimitives.Min(s)` *and* `TensorPrimitives.Max(s)` reads the span twice, whereas `MinMax` folds two running `Vector<T>` accumulators in one pass, so it does the same work for roughly half the memory traffic. This is a **memory-bandwidth win, not a per-element-kernel win** — measured **~1.8× faster** than the two-pass composition on a large out-of-cache span (1,000,000 `int`), a wash (~15% slower) on a small in-cache span (1,024 `int`) where the BCL's heavily-tuned AVX-512 kernels dominate; the docs say so plainly. It is exact for the four integer element types (no NaN ambiguity) and throws `ArgumentException` on an empty span; `float` / `double` are intentionally excluded because correct NaN-propagation semantics deserve an explicit policy rather than whatever `Vector.Min` / `Vector.Max` happen to do. (2) `CheckedSum(ReadOnlySpan<int>)` is an **overflow-checked** integer sum that fills a correctness gap: `TensorPrimitives.Sum` wraps silently and a scalar `checked` loop cannot vectorize (the per-element check has a side effect), so `CheckedSum` widens each `int` lane to `long` — the SIMD accumulation provably cannot overflow for any reachable span — and range-checks **only** the final narrowing to `int`, throwing `OverflowException` on a true overflow (the returned value is therefore always the exact mathematical sum, never a wrapped one); an empty span sums to `0`. Measured **~4.6× faster** than the scalar `checked` loop at 1,024 elements (~3.2× at 1,000,000), i.e. it beats the only *safe* BCL option on speed while staying correct where the fast `TensorPrimitives.Sum` wraps. Both methods use portable `Vector<T>` with a scalar tail (accelerate where SIMD exists, correct everywhere), are allocation-free, and AOT-safe. The #197 spike's third candidate, an integer **histogram / bincount**, was evaluated and **deliberately not shipped**: its only BCL alternative is LINQ `GroupBy().Count()`, the win is purely allocation avoidance, a one-line `counts[values[i]]++` loop covers it without a named primitive, and the scatter pattern does not vectorize portably — closing it as "the BCL (a plain loop) covers it" per the guiding rule.
- `SimdReductionsTests` (`Celerity.Tests/Utils`) — correctness coverage reconciled against scalar oracles. `MinMax` is pinned on small exact cases (single element, negatives, extrema seeded at the very ends so the horizontal lane reduction must surface a first- and last-lane value) and reconciled against a scalar min/max oracle for `int` and `long` across a **vector-boundary length sweep** (1, 2, 3, 7, 8, 15, 16, 17, 31, 64, 257, 1000 — sub-vector spans, exact multiples, and ragged tails where SIMD off-by-ones hide), plus the empty-span `ArgumentException` for all four element types and the `uint` / `ulong` extrema. `CheckedSum` covers the empty-is-`0` case, small signed/positive sums, a length-sweep reconciliation against a `long` oracle, the positive- and negative-overflow `OverflowException` paths, an exactly-`int.MaxValue` boundary that must *not* throw, and the path where the SIMD long accumulation succeeds and only the final checked cast catches the overflow.
- `SimdReductionsBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks` — times the fused/checked helpers against the BCL composition they replace, one `BenchmarkCategory` each (`MinMax` / `CheckedSum`) over `[Params(1024, 1_000_000)]` (an in-cache and an out-of-cache size, so the single-pass memory-traffic win is visible). The `MinMax` baseline is the two-pass `TensorPrimitives.Min` + `TensorPrimitives.Max`, alongside a naive scalar loop and the `SimdReductions` candidate; the `CheckedSum` baseline is the scalar `checked` loop (the only safe BCL way), with the silently-wrapping `TensorPrimitives.Sum` shown for reference as the unchecked speed ceiling. The benchmark project gains a `System.Numerics.Tensors` `9.0.0` `PackageReference` purely for these BCL baselines (the project is `IsPackable=false`, so this adds no dependency to any shipped package). It is an isolated microbenchmark, so it is registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s `ExtendedBenchmarks` array (the weekly `--ci-extended` run and the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard), not the per-PR core regression gate — mirroring `FastModBenchmark` / `PrngBenchmark` / `VarIntBenchmark` / `CountDigitsBenchmark` / `GuidBenchmark` / `SpanBitsBenchmark`.
- Documentation for `SimdReductions`: a new "SimdReductions (fused / specialized SIMD reductions)" section in `docs/api/utilities.md` (the `TensorPrimitives`-already-covers-those framing, the full signature surface, the single-pass-vs-two-pass and widening-checked-sum rationale with the measured ~1.8× / ~4.6× numbers and the explicit in-cache caveat, a runnable example, the empty-span / NaN-scope / exactness contract, and the histogram-not-shipped note) and a new `SimdReductions` paragraph in the README's "Primitives" section; the `Celerity.Primitives` package `<Description>` now mentions the fused SIMD reductions (the `simd` package tag was already present). The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises `MinMax` (`int` / `long` / `uint` extrema seeded at the vector ends) and `CheckedSum` (a matching-arithmetic case and the overflow-throws path), so the Native AOT publish job compiles the `Vector<T>` `Min`/`Max` fold, the `Vector.Widen` accumulation, and the checked narrowing. (Parity note: the cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites and the `web/dev/bench` `COLLECTIONS` / "What ships in the box" dashboard arrays key on *collection* families benchmarked vs a BCL collection — span reductions are neither a keyed collection nor a `HashSet`/`Dictionary`-baselined entry, so neither applies, exactly as for `FastUtils.FastMod` / `CountDigits`, the struct PRNG suite, `VarInt`, `FastGuid`, the alignment helpers, and `SpanBits`. The benchmark is baselined against `TensorPrimitives`, not a Celerity collection, so it has no `COLLECTIONS` dashboard card either.)
- `FastUtils.AlignUp` / `AlignDown` / `IsAligned` (`int` / `long` / `nuint` overloads) and `SpanBits` (in the `Celerity.Primitives` namespace) — power-of-two **alignment helpers** and a non-owning **span bit-packing** helper, the sixth of the milestone 2.1.0 `Celerity.Primitives` work ([#196](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/196)). The alignment helpers round a size or a (pointer-sized) address to a power-of-two boundary — sub-allocating from a buffer, padding a struct stride to a SIMD width, finding the start of the cache line / page a pointer sits in, or sizing a backing array in machine words. The arithmetic is the classic mask trick (`AlignUp = (v + (a - 1)) & ~(a - 1)`, `AlignDown = v & ~(a - 1)`, `IsAligned = (v & (a - 1)) == 0`), which the BCL keeps in an `internal` `Align` helper rather than expose; `FastUtils` exposes it for `int` / `long` sizes and pointer-sized `nuint` addresses. The `alignment` must be a power of two — validated with `BitOperations.IsPow2`, so a non-power-of-two (including `0`, and for the signed widths a negative) throws `ArgumentOutOfRangeException`; an already-aligned value is returned unchanged (`AlignDown <= value <= AlignUp` always holds), and `AlignUp` can overflow within `alignment - 1` of the type maximum exactly as the underlying `+` would (a documented call-site concern). `SpanBits` is the **non-owning counterpart to `BitSet`**: where `BitSet` is a length-tracking collection that *owns* its backing array, `SpanBits` is a thin set of static operations over **caller-owned** bit storage — a `Span<ulong>` / `ReadOnlySpan<ulong>` of 64-bit words you already manage (a `stackalloc` buffer, a slice of a larger array, a pooled / rented buffer). `System.Collections.BitArray`, the other BCL option, is a heap class with no span access, no population count, and no set-bit scan. The surface is `WordCount(bitCount)` (`ceil(bitCount / 64)`, for sizing the span), `Get` / `Set` / `Clear` / `Flip` (single bit; `Flip` returns the bit's new value, matching `BitSet.Flip`), `PopCount` (total set bits via hardware `POPCNT` per word), and `NextSetBit(bits, fromIndex)` (a forward scan returning the lowest set bit at or after `fromIndex`, or `-1`; it skips whole empty words and uses `TZCNT` within a word, so the scan is proportional to the number of words, not bits). Each bit lives in word `index / 64` at position `index % 64` (least-significant bit first); the single-bit operations index the span directly, so an `index` outside `[0, 64·bits.Length)` throws `IndexOutOfRangeException` from the span access (no silent masking or growth), and `NextSetBit` treats a negative `fromIndex` as `0` and a `fromIndex` at/beyond the end as `-1`. All methods are allocation-free and AOT-safe (no reflection); the alignment helpers, the single-bit `SpanBits` operations, and `WordCount` are `[MethodImpl(AggressiveInlining)]`. Library defaults are unchanged — these are new public primitives on `FastUtils` / `Celerity.Primitives`, alongside `NextPowerOfTwo` / `FastMod` / `FastDiv` / `CountDigits`.
- `AlignmentTests` and `SpanBitsTests` (`Celerity.Tests/Utils`) — the correctness coverage the issue's acceptance asks for. `AlignmentTests` pins `[InlineData]` boundary cases (already-aligned ⇒ unchanged, `alignment == 1` ⇒ everything aligned, `value == 0`) for each width and reconciles `AlignUp` / `AlignDown` / `IsAligned` against a modulo-based reference oracle over 200k randomized `(value, alignment)` pairs per `int` / `long` / `nuint` width (asserting the `AlignDown <= value <= AlignUp` and both-results-aligned invariants), plus the non-power-of-two (`0`, `3`, `6`, `7`, negative) `ArgumentOutOfRangeException` paths. `SpanBitsTests` round-trips `Get` / `Set` / `Clear` / `Flip` across the 31/32/63/64/65/127 **word-boundary** indices, confirms `Set` does not disturb neighbouring bits, exercises empty/full `PopCount`, drives `NextSetBit` across word boundaries (inclusive-of-`fromIndex`, negative-`fromIndex`-as-`0`, past-the-end-as-`-1`), and runs a 200k-operation randomized reconciliation against a plain `bool[]` model (every `Set`/`Clear`/`Flip` mirrored, then `PopCount` and a full `NextSetBit` walk cross-checked against the model), plus the out-of-range `IndexOutOfRangeException` path and the `WordCount` rounding table.
- `SpanBitsBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks` — times the non-owning span helpers against the BCL's heap-allocated `System.Collections.BitArray` for the three operations where a span-of-`ulong` with hardware `POPCNT` / `TZCNT` pulls ahead — population count, a next-set-bit forward scan, and a dense single-bit `Set` loop — one `BenchmarkCategory` each (`PopCount` / `Scan` / `Set`) with the `BitArray` arm as each category's `Baseline`, over `[Params(1024, 65536)]` bit sizes at ~25% density. It is an isolated microbenchmark, so it is registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s `ExtendedBenchmarks` array (the weekly `--ci-extended` run and the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard), not the per-PR core regression gate — mirroring `FastModBenchmark` / `PrngBenchmark` / `VarIntBenchmark` / `CountDigitsBenchmark` / `GuidBenchmark`.
- Documentation for the alignment helpers and `SpanBits`: two new sections in `docs/api/utilities.md` — "Alignment helpers (AlignUp / AlignDown / IsAligned)" (the boundary-rounding rationale, the `internal`-BCL gap, the full `int`/`long`/`nuint` signature surface, a runnable example, and the power-of-two / already-aligned / overflow contract) and "SpanBits (span bit-packing)" (the non-owning-vs-`BitSet` boundary, the `BitArray` comparison, the full surface, a `stackalloc` scratch-bitmap example, and the `WordCount` / direct-index-throws / `NextSetBit` / `Flip` contract) — plus new alignment and `SpanBits` paragraphs in the README's "Primitives" section. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises the alignment helpers across `int` / `long` / `nuint` (incl. the non-power-of-two throw) and `SpanBits` (`Get`/`Set`/`Clear`/`Flip`/`PopCount`/`NextSetBit` over a `stackalloc` bitmap), so the Native AOT publish job compiles the `BitOperations.IsPow2` validation and the `POPCNT`/`TZCNT` bit paths. (Parity note: the cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites and the `web/dev/bench` `COLLECTIONS` / "What ships in the box" dashboard arrays key on *collection* families benchmarked vs a BCL collection — alignment math and a non-owning span helper are neither a keyed collection nor a `HashSet`/`Dictionary`-baselined entry, so neither applies, exactly as for `FastUtils.FastMod` / `CountDigits`, the struct PRNG suite, `VarInt`, and `FastGuid`. The `SpanBits` benchmark is baselined against `System.Collections.BitArray`, not a Celerity collection, so it has no core-suite `COLLECTIONS` card either.)
- `FastGuid` (and `GuidV7Generator<TRng>`) in the `Celerity.Primitives` namespace — fast, allocation-free GUID generation from a struct PRNG: a non-cryptographic **version 4** (`CreateVersion4<TRng>(ref TRng)`, 122 random bits) and an RFC 9562 **version 7** (`CreateVersion7<TRng>(ref TRng, long unixTimeMilliseconds)`, a 48-bit Unix-millisecond timestamp + 74 random bits), the fifth of the milestone 2.1.0 `Celerity.Primitives` work ([#195](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/195)). `Guid.NewGuid()` is RNG-backed (cryptographically random) and comparatively slow; `FastGuid` fills the random bits from a supplied `IRandomSource` struct generator (e.g. `Xoshiro256StarStar`, `WyRand`) instead, so the `NextUInt64` call inlines through the `where TRng : struct, IRandomSource` constraint and the whole loop allocates nothing — the target is several times the throughput of `NewGuid`, for high-rate **trace / correlation / ephemeral IDs** where uniqueness, not unpredictability, is the requirement. **Version 7 is emitted in the RFC 9562 big-endian on-the-wire layout**: the 48-bit timestamp occupies the most-significant bytes in network byte order, so the GUID's canonical string sorts in creation order — the property that keeps database B-tree indexes compact and inserts local. .NET 9's `Guid.CreateVersion7` stores the timestamp in the mixed-endian in-memory `Guid` layout, which scrambles that lexical / database sort order versus the spec (community analysis measured ~35% larger indexes); `FastGuid` constructs the `Guid` via the field constructor with big-endian reads of the first eight bytes so `ToString()` ordering matches time ordering (no dependency on the .NET 9-only `Guid(ReadOnlySpan<byte>, bool bigEndian)` constructor — the library targets net8.0). `GuidV7Generator<TRng>` is a stateful, **strictly monotonic** version 7 generator implementing RFC 9562's monotonic-counter method: it keeps the last timestamp and a 12-bit counter in the `rand_a` field, advancing the counter when the clock has not moved so every GUID in a same-millisecond burst is strictly greater than the last, and borrowing from the next millisecond if the counter is exhausted (>~4096 IDs/ms) — `Next()` stamps `DateTimeOffset.UtcNow`, `Next(long)` takes an explicit clock for tests. Both `CreateVersion4` / `CreateVersion7` always set the correct version nibble (`4` / `7`) and RFC 4122 variant (`10xx`) bits and are deterministic from a seeded generator. **Explicitly NOT cryptographically secure** (and the generator is a not-thread-safe mutable `struct`): the bits come from a PRNG, not `RandomNumberGenerator`, so for unguessable identifiers (security tokens, password-reset links) use `Guid.NewGuid()` instead. Methods are allocation-free and AOT-safe (no reflection); the factories are `[MethodImpl(AggressiveInlining)]`.
- `FastGuidTests` (`Celerity.Tests/Primitives`) — the version/variant + RFC-9562-layout + monotonicity coverage the issue's acceptance asks for, reading each GUID's big-endian bytes back through `ToString("N")` (whose 32 hex digits are exactly the big-endian byte sequence). It asserts the version (`4` / `7`) and variant (`10xx`) bits across 10,000-GUID sweeps; the version 7 timestamp round-trips exactly and **leads the canonical string** (`CreateVersion7(rng, 0x010203040506)` ⇒ `ToString("N")` starts `010203040506`); version 7 sorts by timestamp across milliseconds; the monotonic generator is **strictly increasing** within a single millisecond, across a >4096-per-ms **counter overflow**, when the **wall clock moves backwards**, and across advancing milliseconds (with the embedded timestamp tracked); and both factories and the generator are seed-deterministic and collision-free across 50,000-GUID bursts.
- `GuidBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks` — times `FastGuid` against the BCL GUID factories, one `BenchmarkCategory` per version (`V4` / `V7`) with `Guid.NewGuid()` as each category's `Baseline`, comparing version 4 from `Xoshiro256StarStar` / `WyRand` and version 7 stateless / monotonic-generator forms over a 4,096-GUID loop (each result XOR-folded into a returned accumulator so BenchmarkDotNet cannot dead-code-eliminate the draw). A `Guid.CreateVersion7()` arm is included under `#if NET9_0_OR_GREATER` so the big-endian-vs-mixed-endian v7 comparison can activate; the `Celerity.Benchmarks` host deliberately stays single-target net8.0 (it is a dev tool, not a shipped package — only the three packages multi-target, [#189](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/189)), so this arm is dormant until the benchmark is built for a newer TFM (`dotnet run -f net9.0`/`net10.0`), where that API exists. It is an isolated microbenchmark, so it is registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s `ExtendedBenchmarks` array (the weekly `--ci-extended` run and the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard), not the per-PR core regression gate — mirroring `FastModBenchmark` / `PrngBenchmark` / `VarIntBenchmark`.
- Documentation for `FastGuid`: a new "FastGuid (fast non-crypto GUID v4 / v7)" section in `docs/api/utilities.md` (the not-crypto warning, the full signature surface, the big-endian-vs-`Guid.CreateVersion7` database-sort rationale, the monotonic-generator method, runnable v4/v7/monotonic examples, and the version/variant/timestamp/determinism contract) and a new `FastGuid` paragraph in the README's "Primitives" section. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises `CreateVersion4` / `CreateVersion7` (version/variant bits + big-endian timestamp prefix + burst distinctness) and the `GuidV7Generator` same-millisecond monotonicity across counter overflow, so the Native AOT publish job compiles the ref-generic factory instantiations and the mutable monotonic-counter struct. (Parity note: the cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites and the `web/dev/bench` `COLLECTIONS` / "What ships in the box" dashboard arrays key on *collection* families benchmarked vs a BCL collection — a stateless ID-generation primitive is neither a keyed collection nor a `HashSet`/`Dictionary`-baselined entry, so neither applies, exactly as for `FastUtils.FastMod`, the struct PRNG suite, and `VarInt`.)
- `FastUtils.CountDigits` (`uint` / `ulong` / `int` / `long` overloads) and `FastUtils.Log10` (`uint` / `ulong`) — a public base-10 **digit counter** and its companion integer logarithm, the fourth of the milestone 2.1.0 `Celerity.Primitives` work ([#194](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/194)). The number of decimal digits of an integer is exactly what you need to size a buffer before `TryFormat`, align a fixed-width numeric column, or pre-measure log / CSV / JSON output. The BCL has a fast LZCNT-based counter (`System.Buffers.Text.FormattingHelpers.CountDigits`) but keeps it `internal`; the only public base-10 log is the floating-point `Math.Log10`, which is slower and **mis-rounds at exact powers of ten** (rounding can make `(int)Math.Log10(1000)` come out `2`). `CountDigits(uint)` uses Lemire's [digit-count algorithm](https://lemire.me/blog/2021/06/03/computing-the-number-of-digits-of-an-integer-even-faster/): a single `BitOperations.Log2` (one LZCNT) indexes a 32-entry magic table whose value, added to `value` and shifted right by 32, is the digit count — no branches, no division; `CountDigits(ulong)` reduces the value to its top decimal group with at most one division and finishes with a short comparison ladder. Both are exact across the whole range (`1` for `0`, up to `10` for `uint.MaxValue` and `20` for `ulong.MaxValue`). The signed `int` / `long` overloads count the **magnitude** only (the sign is excluded, so `CountDigits(-5) == 1`), computing the magnitude by unsigned two's-complement negation so `int.MinValue` / `long.MinValue` are handled without overflow (unlike `Math.Abs`). `Log10(value)` is `CountDigits(value) - 1` — exact at every power of ten where the floating-point `Math.Log10` can round the wrong way; `log10(0)` is mathematically undefined and documented to return `0`. We deliberately do **not** try to beat `int.ToString` / `TryFormat` itself (already optimized) — only the digit-count primitive the BCL keeps internal. The library defaults are unchanged — this is a new public primitive on `FastUtils`, alongside `NextPowerOfTwo` / `FastMod` / `FastDiv`. The 32-bit and signed counters and both `Log10` overloads are `[MethodImpl(AggressiveInlining)]`, allocation-free (the magic table is a one-time `static readonly` init, not a per-call allocation), and AOT-safe (no reflection).
- `CountDigitsTests` (`Celerity.Tests/Utils`) — the correctness coverage the issue's acceptance asks for, reconciling every result against `value.ToString().Length`. It runs two **dense exhaustive** sweeps (`uint` and `ulong` over `[0, 2,000,000)`) that catch any off-by-one a random sample skips, pins every power-of-ten boundary and its neighbours via `[InlineData]` for both widths (`9/10`, `99/100`, … up to `uint.MaxValue` / `ulong.MaxValue`, including the 64-bit path's `10^7` and `10^14` internal division boundaries), adds ~200k random values per width plus an every-`10^k`-and-neighbours loop across the full range, covers the signed magnitude-only contract (`-5 → 1`, `int.MinValue → 10`, `long.MinValue → 19` with no overflow) against `Math.Abs((long)value)` and a 200k random sweep per signed width, and verifies `Log10` is `CountDigits - 1` exhaustively over the low range and at every power of ten across both widths (the case the floating-point `Math.Log10` mis-rounds).
- `CountDigitsBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks` — times `CountDigits` against the two obvious alternatives, a naive divide-by-ten loop (each category's `Baseline`) and a floating-point `(int)Math.Log10(v) + 1`, at 32- and 64-bit width over a 4,096-value buffer, one `BenchmarkCategory` per width (`Digits32` / `Digits64`). It is an isolated microbenchmark, so it is registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s `ExtendedBenchmarks` array (the weekly `--ci-extended` run and the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard), not the per-PR core regression gate — mirroring `FastModBenchmark` / `PrngBenchmark` / `VarIntBenchmark`.
- Documentation for `CountDigits` / `Log10`: a new "CountDigits / Log10 (base-10 digit count)" section in `docs/api/utilities.md` (the buffer-sizing rationale, the `internal`-BCL / floating-point-`Math.Log10` gap, the full signature surface, the Lemire LZCNT-table / comparison-ladder algorithms, a runnable size-then-`TryFormat` example, and the zero-counts-as-one / signed-magnitude-only / `MinValue` / `Log10(0)` contract) and a new `CountDigits` paragraph in the README's "Primitives" section pointing at it. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises `CountDigits` across all four overloads (a 50k-value exhaustive 32-bit cross-check against `ToString().Length`, 64-bit boundary samples incl. `ulong.MaxValue`, the signed magnitude-only + `int`/`long` `MinValue` cases) and `Log10` at the power-of-ten boundaries, so the Native AOT publish job compiles the `BitOperations.Log2` and table-lookup paths. (Parity note: the cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites and the `web/dev/bench` `COLLECTIONS` / "What ships in the box" dashboard arrays key on *collection* families benchmarked vs a BCL collection — a stateless math primitive is neither a keyed collection nor a `HashSet`/`Dictionary`-baselined entry, so neither applies, exactly as for `FastUtils.FastMod` / `NextPowerOfTwo`, the struct PRNG suite, and `VarInt`.)
- `VarInt` in the `Celerity.Primitives` namespace — a span-based **variable-length integer codec**: LEB128 for unsigned `uint` / `ulong` and zig-zag + LEB128 for signed `int` / `long`, the third of the milestone 2.1.0 `Celerity.Primitives` work ([#193](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/193)). A varint stores a small magnitude in fewer bytes than its fixed width — each byte carries 7 payload bits and a continuation flag (`0x80`) in its high bit, least-significant group first — the wire format Protocol Buffers, the .NET metadata tables, and most custom binary serializers use for length prefixes and field tags. The BCL exposes this **only** as `BinaryWriter.Write7BitEncodedInt` / `BinaryReader.Read7BitEncodedInt`, bound to a `Stream` and allocating a writer/reader (runtime [#24473](https://github.com/dotnet/runtime/issues/24473)); there is no public span overload. `VarInt` fills that gap for no-stream / no-allocation hot paths (custom wire codecs, packet builders, append-only logs, serializers that own their byte buffer), encoding directly over a caller-owned `Span<byte>` / `ReadOnlySpan<byte>`. **Signed values are zig-zag encoded** (`(n << 1) ^ (n >> (W-1))`, decoded branchlessly) so small magnitudes of either sign stay short — a naive LEB128 of `-1` is always the full 10 bytes, whereas zig-zag maps `0,-1,1,-2,… → 0,1,2,3,…`; the `int` / `long` overloads apply it automatically while the `uint` / `ulong` overloads are plain LEB128. The public surface is `TryWriteVarInt(Span<byte>, value, out int bytesWritten)` / `TryReadVarInt(ReadOnlySpan<byte>, out value, out int bytesRead)` for all four widths, the `VarIntLength(value)` size helper (computed via `BitOperations.LeadingZeroCount`, exact against the bytes a write reports), the `MaxVarIntLength32` (5) / `MaxVarIntLength64` (10) ceilings for buffer sizing, and the standalone `ZigZagEncode` / `ZigZagDecode` transforms. Every `TryWrite` / `TryRead` is **bounds-safe and never throws**: a write into a too-small destination returns `false` with `0` bytes (leaving nothing partial), and a read of a truncated source (continuation bit with no further byte) or a malformed/overflowing one (more than the width's maximum length, or a final byte whose bits exceed the target width — an `int`'s 5th byte above `0x0F`, a `ulong`'s 10th byte above `0x01`) returns `false` with `0` bytes. Overload selection is by argument type, so callers use an explicit `out uint` / `out int` (not `out var`) and a `u` / `UL` suffix for unsigned literals. All methods are allocation-free and AOT-safe (no reflection); the transforms and length helpers are `[MethodImpl(AggressiveInlining)]`.
- `VarIntTests` (`Celerity.Tests/Utils`) — the round-trip fuzz the issue's acceptance asks for. It sweeps representative per-width values (the 1-/2-/3-/4-/5-/.../10-byte length boundaries, the type extremes, the sign-bit boundary) plus ~5,000 random values for each of `uint` / `ulong` / `int` / `long`, asserting both the value and the `bytesWritten == bytesRead == VarIntLength` accounting; two **dense exhaustive** sweeps (`uint` over `[0, 2,000,000)` and `int` over `[-1,000,000, 1,000,000)`) that catch any off-by-one a random sample skips; a pinned LEB128-layout check (`0 → 0x00`, `127 → 0x7F`, `128 → 0x80 0x01`, `300 → 0xAC 0x02`); the `VarIntLength` size helper against an `[InlineData]` table and a 20k random sweep reconciled with actual bytes written; the zig-zag transforms against an `[InlineData]` table (incl. `int.MinValue → 0xFFFFFFFF`, `long.MinValue → ulong.MaxValue`); the short-destination, empty-destination, exactly-fitting, truncated-source, over-length (6-byte `uint` / 11-byte `ulong`), final-byte-overflow (5th byte `> 0x0F` / 10th byte `> 0x01`), and **max-length 10-byte `ulong.MaxValue`** edge cases; and a sequential back-to-back write-then-read over a packed 256-value buffer (the real codec workload) verifying the cursor advances by each varint's length.
- `VarIntBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks` — times the span codec against the BCL's only public varint path, `BinaryWriter.Write7BitEncodedInt64` / `BinaryReader.Read7BitEncodedInt64` over a reused `MemoryStream`, one `BenchmarkCategory` per direction (`Encode` / `Decode`) with the BCL form as each category's `Baseline`, over a 4,096-value buffer biased toward the smaller magnitudes that are the varint sweet spot. The span form encodes straight over a caller-owned buffer with no stream and no allocation, the workload custom serializers actually run. It is an isolated microbenchmark, so it is registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s `ExtendedBenchmarks` array (the weekly `--ci-extended` run and the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard), not the per-PR core regression gate — mirroring `FastModBenchmark` / `PrngBenchmark`.
- Documentation for `VarInt`: a new "VarInt (span varint codec)" section in `docs/api/utilities.md` (the LEB128 / zig-zag rationale, the `Stream`-bound BCL gap, the full signature surface, the workload list, a runnable write/read + packed-delta example, and the overload-selection / bounds-safety / exact-length / special-value contract) and a new `VarInt` paragraph in the README's "Primitives" section pointing at it. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises the unsigned and signed round-trips across the length classes (incl. 10-byte `ulong.MaxValue` and `long.MinValue`) plus the short-buffer and truncated-source failure paths, so the Native AOT publish job compiles the `BitOperations` length path, the write/read loops, and the zig-zag transforms. (Parity note: the cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites and the `web/dev/bench` `COLLECTIONS` / "What ships in the box" dashboard arrays key on *collection* families benchmarked vs a BCL collection — a stateless serialization primitive is neither a keyed collection nor a `HashSet`/`Dictionary`-baselined entry, so neither applies, exactly as for `FastUtils.FastMod` and the struct PRNG suite.)
- An `EqualityComparer<T>.Default` baseline arm in both hasher microbenchmarks — `{Type}_EqualityComparer` per key type in `IntegerHasherBenchmark` (`int` / `long` / `uint` / `ulong` / `Guid`) and `EqualityComparer_Default` in `StringHasherBenchmark`, completing the milestone 1.6.0 honest-positioning work ([#183](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/183)). The existing baseline compared only against the direct `GetHashCode()`; the realistic baseline a .NET developer actually *replaces* is `EqualityComparer<T>.Default.GetHashCode()` — the call a BCL `Dictionary<,>` makes per probe — so each key type is now **bracketed by both**. The comparer is fetched once into a local (mirroring how the dictionary caches it) and the per-call dispatch is left intact, since eliminating that dispatch is precisely what a struct hasher saves. Every arm already XOR-folds its codes into a single returned value, which BenchmarkDotNet consumes, so the loop cannot be dead-code-eliminated — important for the identity / passthrough rows whose "hash" is otherwise a no-op the JIT would fold to a misleading ~0&#160;ns. The class remarks now label the microbenchmarks a **raw-mixing-cost diagnostic only** (the headline metric is end-to-end table throughput) and, for `int` / `long`, label the `{Type}_Identity` / `{Type}_Bcl` rows the **zero-work floor** no mixing hasher can beat. The new arms auto-render on the gh-pages **Hash function throughput** dashboard (the hasher section parses method names by regex, so no `COLLECTIONS` wiring change is needed — that array lists keyed collections, not hashers), with a small `web/dev/bench/index.html` label mapping so the arm reads `EqualityComparer<T>.Default`.
- A curated **struct PRNG suite** in the new `Celerity.Primitives` namespace — `SplitMix64`, `Xoshiro256StarStar`, `Xoroshiro128Plus`, `WyRand`, and `Pcg32`, all implementing a one-method `IRandomSource` interface (`ulong NextUInt64()`), the second of the milestone 2.1.0 `Celerity.Primitives` work ([#192](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/192)). `System.Random` is a heap **class** behind virtual dispatch, `Random.Shared` is not inlinable, and a **seeded** `new Random(seed)` falls back to the legacy Knuth subtractive algorithm — so the determinism path people rely on is both non-xoshiro and slower; there was no public value-type, allocation-free, inlinable PRNG. These are mutable `struct`s that advance their own state, so a tight inner loop allocates nothing and the `NextUInt64` call inlines through a `where TRng : struct, IRandomSource` constraint — the same zero-cost devirtualization the struct hashers use. The suite is curated, no marginal variants (RomuTrio et al. deliberately skipped): `Xoshiro256StarStar` is the general-purpose default (256-bit state, period 2²⁵⁶−1, strong on every output bit — the algorithm .NET uses internally for `Random.Shared`); `Xoroshiro128Plus` is the fastest doubles generator (the `+` scrambler has documented weak *low* bits, so it is consumed only via the high-bit helpers); `WyRand` is the raw-throughput pick (single-word state folded through one 64×64→128-bit `UInt128` multiply, the wyhash core); `SplitMix64` is the canonical seed expander (and usable standalone); `Pcg32` is the statistical-reputation pick with independent **streams** (same seed, different `sequence` ⇒ uncorrelated) and a native 32-bit output (`NextUInt32`; `NextUInt64` concatenates two draws). Every constructor takes an explicit `ulong` seed and is deterministic; the multi-word generators expand the seed through `SplitMix64` so **every** seed is valid (the degenerate all-zero state that would lock xoshiro/xoroshiro is unreachable, including from `seed: 0`). There is no entropy-seeded overload — the suite is deterministic by design. All generators are AOT-safe (no reflection); `NextUInt64` is `[MethodImpl(AggressiveInlining)]` and allocation-free. (Namespace note: `FastUtils` — the existing `Celerity.Primitives` seed — currently lives in the root `Celerity` namespace; the new PRNGs adopt the `Celerity.Primitives` namespace that matches the future package, exactly as the hashers already live under `Celerity.Hashing` ahead of the 2.0.0 package split. Reconciling `FastUtils`'s namespace is part of the package-split work, [#187](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/187).)
- `RandomSourceExtensions` (in `Celerity.Primitives`) — the shared, shape-specific surface built **once** over any `IRandomSource`'s single `NextUInt64` primitive, as `ref this` extension methods constrained to `where TRng : struct, IRandomSource` so each call devirtualizes, inlines, and mutates the generator in place with no boxing: `NextUInt32` (from the strong high bits, safe even for `Xoroshiro128Plus`'s weak low bits), `NextDouble` / `NextSingle` (half-open `[0, 1)` using the full 53- / 24-bit mantissa), `NextBool`, bounded-and-**unbiased** `NextInt(max)` / `NextInt(min, max)` / `NextInt64(min, max)` (Lemire's nearly-divisionless rejection — a single widening multiply on the common path, the rare reject branch only for the residues that would skew the distribution; the full-`int`-range and `>2³²` 64-bit spans handled correctly via `unchecked` magnitude arithmetic), and `NextBytes(Span<byte>)` (eight bytes at a time with a masked tail). The bounded methods throw `ArgumentOutOfRangeException` for a non-positive `maxExclusive` or `maxExclusive < minInclusive`, and return `min` when `min == max`. Because the receiver is taken `ref this`, the helpers require a mutable variable/field (not a temporary), and the constrained-generic shape lets generic algorithms (e.g. a Fisher–Yates shuffle) run zero-cost over any generator.
- `SplitMix64Tests`, `Xoshiro256StarStarTests`, `Xoroshiro128PlusTests`, `WyRandTests`, and `Pcg32Tests` (`Celerity.Tests/Primitives`) — dedicated per-generator suites. Each cross-checks the generator against an **independent reimplementation** of its algorithm over 500 outputs across multiple seeds (validating the struct threads its state correctly — the seeding, the scrambler, and the advance), and asserts seed-0 safety (no stall / non-zero varied stream). `SplitMix64Tests` additionally pins the **published seed-0 reference vector** (Vigna's `splitmix64.c`: `0xE220A8397B1DCDAF`, `0x6E789E6AA1B965F4`, `0x06C45D188009454F`) as an absolute anchor and verifies that copying the struct forks the stream. `Pcg32Tests` adds the XSH-RR reference reimplementation, the `NextUInt64`-concatenates-two-`NextUInt32`-draws contract, the independent-streams property (same seed + different `sequence` ⇒ >99% of outputs differ), and native-32-bit space coverage.
- `RandomSourceContractTests` (`Celerity.Tests/Primitives`) — the cross-generator contract run **identically across the whole family** (the PRNG analog of the cross-collection shared dictionary/set suites): reproducibility (same seed ⇒ identical 1,000-draw stream; distinct seeds differ), no-stall over 2,001 draws from `seed: 0` (>1,990 distinct, catching the classic multi-word-seeding lock-up bug), `NextDouble` / `NextSingle` confined to `[0, 1)` with the sample mean near 0.5 over 100k draws, bounded `NextInt` in range and hitting every bucket of a 6-way die over 60k rolls, `NextInt(min, max)` with a negative lower bound, the `min == max` returns-`min` case, `NextInt64` over a span wider than 2³², the full-`int`-range and full-`long`-range bounds, `NextBytes` filling a 21-byte buffer (two words + a 5-byte tail) reproducibly with an empty-span no-op, `NextBool` balance (48–52% over 100k), and a generic Fisher–Yates shuffle driven through the `where TRng : struct, IRandomSource` path proving it stays a deterministic permutation. Each contract gets per-generator `[Fact]` rows so a failure names the generator while the assertions live once in the generic helpers.
- `PrngBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks` — times every struct generator against `System.Random` (both `Random.Shared` and a **seeded** `new Random(seed)`, the reproducible-yet-slow path the issue calls out) across three workloads — a raw 64-bit draw, a `[0, 1)` double, and a bounded integer — one `BenchmarkCategory` each (`NextULong` / `NextDouble` / `NextBounded`) with the seeded `Random` form as the baseline. It is an isolated microbenchmark, so it is registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s `ExtendedBenchmarks` array (the weekly `--ci-extended` run and the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard), not the per-PR core regression gate — mirroring `FastModBenchmark`.
- Documentation for the struct PRNGs: a new "Struct PRNGs" section in `docs/api/utilities.md` (the `System.Random` gap, the full interface + per-generator + extension-surface signatures, a **generator-selection table** with state/period/use-case, a runnable seed-and-draw + generic-shuffle example, and the determinism / half-open-range / unbiased-bounded / mutable-struct-by-ref / `Pcg32`-specifics / not-thread-safe-not-crypto contract) and a new struct-PRNG paragraph in the README's "Primitives" section pointing at it. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises all five generators through a generic `ExerciseRng<TRng>` helper (determinism, the full derived-range surface, `NextBytes`, and a generic Fisher–Yates shuffle permutation through the constrained-generic path) plus `Pcg32`'s native 32-bit output and independent streams, so the Native AOT publish job compiles each generic instantiation (the SplitMix64 seeding, the `UInt128` wyrand fold, and the Lemire bounded range). (Parity note: the cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites and the `web/dev/bench` `COLLECTIONS` / "What ships in the box" dashboard arrays key on *collection* families benchmarked vs a BCL collection — a value-type PRNG is neither a keyed collection nor a `HashSet`/`Dictionary`-baselined entry, so neither applies, exactly as for `FastUtils.FastMod` / `NextPowerOfTwo`. The family-wide cross-generator coverage is `RandomSourceContractTests` plus the per-generator dedicated suites above.)
- `FastUtils.FastMod` / `FastDiv` / `GetFastModMultiplier` (32-bit `uint` and 64-bit `ulong` overloads) — the first of the milestone 2.1.0 `Celerity.Primitives` work ([#191](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/191)), Daniel Lemire's reciprocal modulo and division (Lemire, Kaser, Kurz, 2019). For a divisor **fixed at run time** and reused across a hot loop, `GetFastModMultiplier(divisor)` precomputes a reciprocal once (`ceil(2^64 / d)` as a `ulong` for the 32-bit overload, `ceil(2^128 / d)` as a `UInt128` for the 64-bit one) so each subsequent `FastMod(value, divisor, multiplier)` / `FastDiv(value, multiplier)` is a widening multiply and a shift instead of a long-latency, non-pipelining hardware `DIV` — the canonical 2–4× win for hash-bucket indexing, ring buffers, sharding, rate limiting, and time-wheel timers. The BCL has exactly this internally (`System.Collections.HashHelpers.GetFastModMultiplier` / `FastMod`) but keeps it `internal`; `FastUtils` exposes it publicly, generalized with a `FastDiv` companion (quotient via the same reciprocal) and the 64-bit variant. The remainder is the high word of `((c·value mod 2^W)·d)` and the quotient is the high word of `(c·value)` (W = 64 or 128); the 32-bit path uses `Math.BigMul`, and the 64-bit path a private `MultiplyHigh(UInt128, ulong)` helper that takes the high 64 bits of the up-to-192-bit product without ever truncating it in a 128-bit container. `FastMod` is exact for every `value` and every `divisor >= 1` (bit-for-bit with `%`); `FastDiv` is exact for every `value` provided the multiplier was produced for a `divisor >= 2` (for `divisor == 1` the multiplier overflows to `0` — still correct for `FastMod`, since every value mod 1 is 0, but `FastDiv` would return `0` instead of the value, a documented call-site guard). `GetFastModMultiplier` throws `ArgumentOutOfRangeException` for a `0` divisor. Both hot-path methods are `[MethodImpl(AggressiveInlining)]`, allocation-free, and AOT-safe (no reflection). The library defaults are unchanged — the Celerity collections still use power-of-two masking (`hash & (size - 1)`), so this is a new public primitive for callers with a non-power-of-two run-time divisor, not a change to any collection's indexing.
- `FastModTests` (`Celerity.Tests/Utils`) — the correctness fuzz the issue's acceptance asks for, reconciling every result against the built-in `%` / `/` operators. It sweeps a representative divisor set (small values, powers of two and their neighbours, primes, and the extreme high end including `uint.MaxValue` / `ulong.MaxValue` and the values just below `2^31` / `2^63`) against boundary dividends (0/1/2, `d·k ± 1` for the first few multiples, the range extremes, the sign-bit boundary) plus ~4,000 full-range random dividends per divisor for both `FastMod` and `FastDiv` at 32- and 64-bit width; a dense **exhaustive** low-range sweep (`value` in `[0, 2,000,000)` for divisors 3 / 7 / 10 / 1000) that catches any off-by-one a random sample could skip; the `divisor == 1` `FastMod`-returns-zero contract; the `GetFastModMultiplier(0)` `ArgumentOutOfRangeException` for both overloads; and that the multiplier equals `ceil(2^W / d)`.
- `FastModBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks` — times `FastMod` / `FastDiv` against the hardware `%` / `/` operators at 32- and 64-bit width, one `BenchmarkCategory` per operation/width (`Mod32` / `Div32` / `Mod64` / `Div64`) with the operator form as each category's `Baseline`. The divisor is held in a `[Params]` field so the JIT treats it as a run-time value and emits a real `DIV` for the baselines (it cannot strength-reduce a non-constant divisor into shifts) — the realistic workload the reciprocal trick targets. It is an isolated microbenchmark, so it is registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s `ExtendedBenchmarks` array (the weekly `--ci-extended` run and the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard), not the per-PR core regression gate.
- Documentation for `FastMod` / `FastDiv`: a new "FastMod / FastDiv" section in `docs/api/utilities.md` (the run-time-fixed-divisor rationale, the full 32-/64-bit signature surface, the workload list, a runnable shard-routing example, and the exactness / `divisor == 1` / zero-divisor contract) and a new "Primitives" section in the README pointing at it. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises `FastUtils.FastMod` / `FastDiv` at both widths (a 50k-value exhaustive 32-bit cross-check against the operators plus 64-bit boundary samples) so the Native AOT publish job compiles the `BigMul` and `UInt128` reciprocal paths. (Parity note: the cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites and the `web/dev/bench` `COLLECTIONS` / "What ships in the box" dashboard arrays key on *collection* families benchmarked vs a BCL collection — a stateless math primitive is neither a keyed collection nor a `HashSet`/`Dictionary`-baselined entry, so neither applies, exactly as for the existing `FastUtils.NextPowerOfTwo`.)
- `ProbeStatisticsEvaluator` / `ProbeStatistics` in `Celerity.Hashing` — a diagnostic that measures what a lookup *actually pays* inside Celerity's open-addressed, power-of-two, **linearly-probed** tables, the second of the milestone 1.6.0 hasher-audit / honest-positioning work ([#182](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/182)). `HashQualityEvaluator` models a *separate-chaining* table (one counter per bucket), so its `MaxBucketLoad` measures how many keys *share* a slot; but the collections do not chain — they linearly probe, which makes adjacent clusters *merge* (primary clustering), so the cost a lookup feels is its **probe length**: the number of slots it reads, from the key's natural slot, before finding the key. `ProbeStatisticsEvaluator.Evaluate<T, THasher>(keys, capacity?, loadFactor = 0.75f)` computes that by replaying the **exact** placement the collections use (`index = hash & (TableSize - 1)`, then linear `(index + 1) & mask` on a collision), sizing the table for the key count *including* load-factor headroom exactly as the bulk constructors do (issue #27), deduplicating keys by `EqualityComparer<T>.Default` to match set semantics, and using a `bool[]` occupancy map (not a sentinel) so it is generic over any key type with no reserved value. The returned `ProbeStatistics` (a `readonly struct` mirroring `HashQualityReport`) carries `KeyCount`, `EntryCount`, `DuplicateKeyCount`, `TableSize`, the achieved `LoadFactor`, `CollisionCount` / `CollisionRate` (entries not in their natural slot — the open-addressing notion, which accounts for primary clustering), `TotalProbeLength`, `AverageProbeLength` (1.0 = every entry in its natural slot), and `MaxProbeLength` (the tail-latency driver). Like `HashQualityEvaluator` it is an offline diagnostic (it materializes the sequence and allocates a table-sized buffer), validates a null `keys` (`ArgumentNullException`, ahead of a bad `loadFactor`), a negative `capacity`, and a `loadFactor` outside `(0, 1)` (`ArgumentOutOfRangeException`), and is AOT-safe (no reflection).
- `ProbeStatisticsEvaluatorTests` — a dedicated suite mirroring `HashQualityEvaluatorTests`: null-source and out-of-range `capacity` / `loadFactor` validation (and null-beats-bad-`loadFactor` ordering), table sizing (key-count-with-headroom → power of two, the `capacity` floor, lower-load-factor-yields-a-larger-table-and-fewer-collisions), the empty-sample neutral report and single-key single-probe report, the identity-hasher-on-a-contiguous-range collision-free case (avg probe exactly 1.0), the constant-hasher triangular probe chain (lengths `1..n`, so total `n(n+1)/2`, max `n`, `n-1` collisions — pinned to closed form), duplicate dedupe accounting, the headline contrast that `Int32Murmur3Hasher` keeps the average probe near 1 on the adversarial key set where `Int32WangNaiveHasher` blows the max probe past 100, determinism, and the generic surface over `string` / `long` keys.
- `HasherEndToEndBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks` — the honest companion to the isolated-`Hash()` `IntegerHasherBenchmark`: it drives each integer hasher (`Int32IdentityHasher` / `Int32WangNaiveHasher` / `Int32WangHasher` / `Int32Murmur3Hasher`) **through `IntDictionary<int, THasher>`** for insert + lookup across all four `Distribution` shapes (uniform / sequential / clustered / adversarial), against the BCL `Dictionary<int, int>` baseline. An isolated `Hash()` loop is misleading — for an `int` key `GetHashCode()` is identity (zero work), so no mixer is ever "faster" in isolation, and the strong hashers earn their cost only by *shortening probe chains*. This benchmark makes that visible: the cheap hashers win on uniform/sequential keys while only the full finalizers survive the adversarial shape. It lives in the **extended** suite (not the per-PR core gate): the adversarial rows are intentionally O(n)-slow, and item counts are capped at `KeyDistributions.MaxAdversarialCount` so the shape stays buildable. Registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s `ExtendedBenchmarks` array so it joins the weekly `--ci-extended` run and the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard.
- `ProbeAnalysisReportRunner` + a `--probe-analysis` switch in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs` — the deterministic, timing-free half of the end-to-end story (mirroring the existing `--hash-quality` report). It sweeps every `int` / `long` hasher across all four distributions via `ProbeStatisticsEvaluator` and prints a GitHub-flavored-markdown probe-length / collision table (10,000 keys per shape at the 0.75 load factor). Because probe geometry is a pure function of (hasher, keys, table size) it does not depend on CI hardware, so the numbers are stable enough to cite directly in the docs.
- Documentation for the probe analysis: a new "End-to-end probe analysis" section in `docs/api/hashing.md` (the chaining-vs-probing distinction, the `ProbeStatisticsEvaluator` / `ProbeStatistics` surface and member table, and a runnable example), a new "Measure probe length, not just `Hash()` speed" subsection in `docs/performance.md` with the **measured 10k-key probe table** (showing identity/naive *perfect* on sequential keys, the naive fold collapsing on clustered/adversarial keys, and the strong finalizers holding near 1.75 — plus the caveat that identity passing the adversarial set is an artifact of a construction targeted at the naive fold, *not* a HashDoS defence), a `HasherEndToEndBenchmark` row in the extended-suite table, and README updates (the benchmarks section and the custom-hashing diagnostic note now point to probe length / `ProbeStatisticsEvaluator`). (Parity note: the cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites and the `web/dev/bench` `COLLECTIONS` arrays key on *collection* families, not a diagnostic/hasher API, so neither applies — exactly as for the analogous `HashQualityEvaluator`, which likewise has only a dedicated test and no dashboard `COLLECTIONS` entry or `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` exercise.)
- `Int32IdentityHasher` / `Int64IdentityHasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — explicit **zero-work** pass-through integer hashers, the first of the milestone 1.6.0 hasher-audit / honest-positioning work ([#185](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/185)). `Int32IdentityHasher.Hash(key)` returns `key` unchanged; `Int64IdentityHasher.Hash(key)` returns `(int)key` (the low 32 bits). They are the labelled **floor** of the cost-vs-avalanche ladder: because `int.GetHashCode()` is itself the identity function, no mixing hasher can beat identity on raw throughput, which is exactly the point — the real value of the struct hashers is distribution quality / determinism / the zero-cost devirtualized generic, not hashing speed. They exist as an explicit opt-out from mixing (the F14 / ahash / FxHash position): when keys are already uniform and trusted (dense sequential IDs, contiguous indices), any avalanche step is pure overhead, and identity is collision-free on any contiguous range. The documented **decision rule**: uniform/trusted keys → skip mixing (identity); clustered / adversarial / (for `long`) high-half-distinct keys → mix (`Int32WangHasher`/`Int64WangHasher` full Thomas-Wang finalizer, or the Murmur3 finalizers). Two caveats are documented on both types: Celerity's open-addressed, power-of-two-masked tables are *more* sensitive to a weak integer hash than the prime-bucketed BCL `Dictionary<,>` (so identity is the right call only when the *low* bits are well distributed — and `Int64IdentityHasher` collides keys that differ only in the upper 32 bits, by design), and a fixed identity (or any fixed-seed) hasher is **not** a HashDoS defence. The library defaults are unchanged — `IntDictionary` / `LongDictionary` / `IntSet` / `LongSet` keep `Int32WangNaiveHasher` / `Int64WangNaiveHasher`; identity is an opt-in floor, not a new default. AOT-safe; `Hash` is allocation-free and `[MethodImpl(AggressiveInlining)]`.
- `Int32IdentityHasherTests` and `Int64IdentityHasherTests` — dedicated suites mirroring the existing integer-hasher tests. They cover the pass-through identity (the int hasher equals `int.GetHashCode()`; the long hasher equals `(int)key`), determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances, the by-design upper-32-bit truncation collision on `Int64IdentityHasher` (a pinned-down contract, not a bug), collision-freedom on a contiguous range, end-to-end integration through `CelerityDictionary` / `IntDictionary` / `LongDictionary` / `CeleritySet` / `IntSet` / `LongSet` (including the out-of-band zero-key slot, where `Hash(0) == 0 == EMPTY_KEY` but the table stores key 0 out-of-band so it never aliases the sentinel), and a dense 5k sequential-key fill that round-trips with zero mixing ops.
- `IntegerHasherBenchmark` rows `Int32_Identity` / `Int64_Identity` in `Celerity.Benchmarks`, run through the same JIT-devirtualized `where THasher : struct, IHashProvider<T>` path as the other hasher rows. The class remarks label them the **zero-work floor**, expected to track the `*_Bcl` baseline (identity = `int.GetHashCode()`), making the honest "no mixer beats identity on speed" point visible on the gh-pages **Hash function throughput** dashboard. The `{Type}_{Hasher}` method-name convention keeps the dashboard grouping intact, so no `web/dev/bench` wiring change is needed (hashers have no `COLLECTIONS` dashboard entry — that array lists keyed collections, not hashers).
- Documentation for the identity hashers: `Int32IdentityHasher` / `Int64IdentityHasher` sections in `docs/api/hashing.md` (the zero-work-floor framing, the identity / low-32-bits algorithms, the skip-vs-mix decision rule, the open-addressed-table sensitivity caveat, and the not-a-HashDoS-defence caveat), two new "Alternative" entries in that file's "Choosing a hasher" table, an updated `int` / `long` row in the README "Choosing a hasher" table, and a new README note repositioning the hasher value proposition toward distribution/determinism over speed. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises `IntDictionary<string, Int32IdentityHasher>` / `LongDictionary<string, Int64IdentityHasher>` (out-of-band zero key) and `IntSet<Int32IdentityHasher>` / `LongSet<Int64IdentityHasher>` (dense sequential fill) so the Native AOT publish compiles the new generic instantiations. (The cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites do not apply — those key on *collection* families, not hasher families; a new hasher's cross-cutting coverage is the shared `IntegerHasherBenchmark` plus the dictionary/set integration tests above.)
- `CountMinSketch<T, THasher>` in `Celerity.Collections` — a space-efficient **probabilistic frequency estimator** (estimates how many times each element has been added), the fourth of the milestone 2.0.0 specialized-collections work ([#30](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/30)) and the frequency-counting third of the streaming-sketch trio alongside the membership-testing `BloomFilter<T, THasher>` and the cardinality-estimating `HyperLogLog<T, THasher>`. Where a `Dictionary<TKey, int>` frequency table must store every distinct key to count it — memory proportional to the distinct-key count — a Count-Min sketch stores only a fixed `depth × width` grid of counters (a few KB) that **never grows with the data**, trading exactness for a bounded *one-sided* error: `EstimateCount` **never underestimates** an element's true frequency, and with probability at least `1 − delta` overestimates by no more than `epsilon · TotalCount` (Cormode & Muthukrishnan, 2005). Each element maps to one counter per row; `Add` increments those `depth` counters and `EstimateCount` returns the **minimum** across them — because every touched counter accumulates the element's full count plus only non-negative collision contributions, the minimum is the tightest of the `depth` overestimates and can never fall below the truth. The sketch sizes itself from two error parameters: the relative error factor `epsilon` drives the per-row width `w = ceil(e / epsilon)` (rounded **up to a power of two** so a column index is a mask rather than a modulo; `w ≥ 4` always since `epsilon < 1`), and the failure probability `delta` drives the row count `d = ceil(ln(1 / delta))` (floored at one row via `Math.Max` so a `delta` so close to 1 that `1/delta` rounds to `1.0` still yields a usable sketch). The `d` counter columns for an element are derived from a **single** `IHashProvider<T>` call by **double hashing** (Kirsch–Mitzenmacher): the 32-bit base hash is avalanched into 64 bits (a SplitMix64 finalizer) whose two halves seed the recurrence `g_i = h1 + i·h2` (the stride forced odd so the rows spread out), so adding rows costs arithmetic, not more `Hash()` calls. Because it stores only counters there is no empty-slot sentinel, so unlike the hash-table collections it needs **no out-of-band handling** for `default(T)` (a zero `int`, `Guid.Empty`, the empty string are ordinary elements); a `null` reference is mapped to a fixed base hash so the hasher (the string hashers throw on `null`) is never invoked with `null`, via a `typeof(T).IsValueType`-guarded check the JIT folds away for value-type instantiations (no boxing). Public surface: `Add(item)`, `Add(item, count)` (weighted, throwing `ArgumentOutOfRangeException` on a non-positive count), `EstimateCount`, `UnionWith` (in-place elementwise counter merge of two equally-sized sketches, so the result is exactly the sketch adding both streams to one would have produced), `Clear`, the `Width` / `Depth` / `Epsilon` / `Delta` / `TotalCount` properties, the `DEFAULT_EPSILON` / `DEFAULT_DELTA` constants, and two constructors (`(epsilon, delta)` and `(IEnumerable<T>, epsilon, delta)` with null-source-beats-bad-parameters validation ordering). There is deliberately **no `Remove`** — decrementing a counter could push an unrelated element's estimate below its true frequency, breaking the never-underestimate guarantee. Documented as the pick for per-element frequency estimation over large/unbounded streams (heavy hitters / top-k, approximate per-key counts, rate limiting, deduplicated frequency counts merged across shards) rather than a `Dictionary` frequency-table replacement. AOT-safe (no reflection); `Add` / `EstimateCount` are allocation-free.
- `CountMinSketchTests`, `CountMinSketchAccuracyTests`, and `CountMinSketchCollisionTests` — dedicated suites. The functional tests cover the never-underestimate semantics over a 5k random fill, empty-estimates-zero, the weighted `Add(item, count)` overload (and its non-positive-count `ArgumentOutOfRangeException`), `TotalCount` tracking, `default(int)` / `Guid.Empty` / empty-string as ordinary elements, the out-of-band `null`-reference path (a `StringFnV1AHasher` sketch that would otherwise throw), `Clear` (and the already-empty no-op), power-of-two `Width` / positive `Depth` sizing, smaller-`epsilon`-widens-rows / smaller-`delta`-adds-rows, the `delta`-rounds-to-one floor keeping `Depth ≥ 1`, the `IEnumerable` ctor (per-occurrence counting, non-collection source, empty source, parameter honoring, null-source `ArgumentNullException` including beating a bad `epsilon`), full constructor validation (`epsilon` / `delta` each `<= 0` / `>= 1` / negative / `> 1` / `NaN`), and `UnionWith` (frequency merge, other-left-unmodified, null, incompatible-`Width` and incompatible-`Depth` `ArgumentException`); the accuracy suite **statistically** asserts the realized error stays within `epsilon · TotalCount` for all but a `delta` fraction of keys across `epsilon ∈ {0.1, 0.01, 0.001}` on a skewed 200k-element stream (deterministic seed) with the hard never-underestimate check on every key, a heavy-hitter test (3 high-frequency keys in 100k of noise estimated within bound), string and long never-underestimate fills, and an absent-key test (estimates are non-negative and almost always exactly zero); the collision suite drives a degenerate constant hasher (every element shares one set of counters) to prove estimates gracefully inflate to the total count (a maximal overestimate, never an underestimate or a crash), a negative-constant `int.MinValue` base hash for in-range column derivation, and the loose-`delta` minimum-one-row case. A `CountMinSketch` arm was also added to the differential testing layer: a **one-directional** CsCheck model property test (`CollectionModelPropertyTests.CountMinSketch_ShouldNeverUnderestimate_VsBclFrequencyTable`) and a `Celerity.Fuzz` target (`CountMinSketch`, registered in `Differential.All`), both reconciling that every element's estimate is at least its exact count in a `Dictionary<int, long>` frequency-table oracle (overestimates are not reconciled — a collision may legitimately inflate an estimate).
- `CountMinSketchBenchmark` in `Celerity.Benchmarks`, comparing `CountMinSketch<int, Int32WangNaiveHasher>` against a `Dictionary<int, int>` exact frequency-table baseline at 1k / 100k items across `Add` (building each structure from a skewed stream) and `Estimate` (`EstimateCount` vs the `Dictionary` indexer on a pre-built structure). Like the other sketch benchmarks it enables `[MemoryDiagnoser]` because the headline is allocation — the `Allocated` column shows the sketch building in a fixed counter-grid footprint that does not grow with the distinct-key count, versus a `Dictionary` storing every distinct key. The `[Params]` field is named `ItemCount` so the dashboard's benchmark-name parser picks it up, and the baseline methods are named `Dictionary_*` so they parse as the existing `Dictionary` `BCL_TYPES` entry. Registered in `Celerity.Benchmarks/Program.cs`'s core `--ci` `CoreBenchmarks` array so it joins the `RunAllJoined` CI report and the gh-pages dashboard.
- Benchmark dashboard wiring for `CountMinSketch`: a "What ships in the box" ship card in `web/index.html`, and a `COLLECTIONS` entry (key / title / vs `Dictionary<int, int>` / ops `['Add', 'Estimate']`) in both `web/dev/bench/index.html` and `web/dev/bench/detail.html` so the auto-published `data.js` is surfaced rather than ignored. The baseline series parses as the existing `Dictionary` `BCL_TYPES` entry, so no `BCL_TYPES` change is needed.
- Documentation for `CountMinSketch`: a full section in `docs/api/collections.md` (the fixed-small-memory / one-sided-never-underestimate / add-and-estimate contract, the counter-grid / per-row-counter / minimum-estimate algorithm and single-hasher-call double-hashing derivation, the `epsilon` / `delta` sizing, constructors and validation ordering, the full method/property surface, default-element and out-of-band-`null` handling, when to pick it over a `Dictionary` frequency table and how it differs from `BloomFilter` / `HyperLogLog`, and a runnable per-URL-frequency + shard-merge example) and README updates — the Collections list, the "Choosing a collection" decision table, and a new "estimate per-element frequencies in a fixed few KB" Quick start subsection. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` now exercises `CountMinSketch<int, Int32WangNaiveHasher>` (weighted / unweighted add / never-underestimate over a 50k skewed fill / power-of-two width / `UnionWith` / `Clear`), `CountMinSketch<string, StringFnV1AHasher>` (the `IEnumerable` ctor and the out-of-band `null` element), and `CountMinSketch<Guid, GuidHasher>` (the `Guid.Empty` element) so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiations, the SplitMix64 avalanche, and the double-hashing column probe. (The cross-collection shared dictionary/set test suites do not apply — like `BloomFilter` / `HyperLogLog` / `BitSet`, a Count-Min sketch is not a keyed dictionary or set and shares none of those `Add`/`TryAdd`/constructor/`IReadOnlyDictionary` rows.)
- `HyperLogLog<T, THasher>` in `Celerity.Collections` — a space-efficient **probabilistic cardinality estimator** (counts the number of *distinct* elements added), the third of the milestone 2.0.0 specialized-collections work ([#30](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/30)) and the counting companion to the membership-testing `BloomFilter<T, THasher>`. Where a `HashSet<T>` must store every distinct element to count them — memory proportional to the cardinality — a HyperLogLog stores only `m = 2^precision` one-byte registers (16&#160;KB at the default precision 14) that **never grow with the data**, trading exactness for a bounded *relative* standard error of ≈ `1.04 / sqrt(m)` (~0.81% at the default precision), whether it counts a thousand distinct elements or a billion. Each element is hashed to 64 bits, routed to a register by its top `precision` bits, and the register records the largest **rank** (one plus the leading-zero count) seen in the remaining bits — a sentinel bit planted just below the remaining bit field keeps the rank in `[1, 64 − precision + 1]`; `EstimateCardinality()` then takes the bias-corrected harmonic mean of `2^register` across all registers (Flajolet et al., 2007), applying the standard small-range **linear counting** correction when the raw estimate is small and registers remain zero (no large-range correction is needed — the 64-bit hash space dwarfs any realistic cardinality, and an empty estimator returns `0` exactly). The 64-bit hash is derived from a **single** `IHashProvider<T>` call by avalanching the 32-bit base hash with the SplitMix64 finalizer (a bijection on 64 bits, so distinct base hashes stay distinct — no collisions beyond the hasher's own), so any existing hasher plugs in unchanged and `Add` costs one `Hash()` call. Because it stores only register ranks there is no empty-slot sentinel, so unlike the hash-table collections it needs **no out-of-band handling** for `default(T)` (a zero `int`, `Guid.Empty`, the empty string are ordinary elements); a `null` reference is mapped to a fixed base hash so the hasher (the string hashers throw on `null`) is never invoked with `null`, via a `typeof(T).IsValueType`-guarded check the JIT folds away for value-type instantiations (no boxing). Public surface: `Add`, `EstimateCardinality`, `UnionWith` (in-place per-register-max merge of two equal-precision estimators, so the result estimates the **union** cardinality with no extra error — unlike a Bloom-filter union), `Clear`, the `Precision` / `RegisterCount` / `StandardError` properties, the `DEFAULT_PRECISION` / `MIN_PRECISION` / `MAX_PRECISION` constants, and two constructors (`(precision)` and `(IEnumerable<T>, precision)` with null-source-beats-bad-precision validation ordering). There is deliberately **no `Remove`** and no per-element membership test — it tracks only the aggregate distinct count. Documented as the pick for distinct counting over large/unbounded streams (unique visitors / events, distinct-value cardinality, deduplicated counts merged across shards) rather than a `HashSet` replacement. AOT-safe (no reflection); `Add` is allocation-free.
- `HyperLogLogTests`, `HyperLogLogAccuracyTests`, and `HyperLogLogCollisionTests` — dedicated suites.
