a twenty-one-case spec-equivalence theory asserting the hasher equals a canonical, independently-written byte-oriented `metrohash64_1` over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(...)` folded to 32 bits (using `BinaryPrimitives.ReadUInt64/UInt32/UInt16LittleEndian` and even the canonical single-byte tail step the hasher can elide on an always-even UTF-16 stream, so it shares no code with the hasher's char-loop and pins the 32-byte stripe / 16-byte-block / 8-byte-lane / 4-byte-block / 2-byte-char tail boundaries, the post-loop lattice, byte order, the finalization, and the final fold) across ASCII / mixed / CJK / embedded-NUL / surrogate-pair inputs and lengths that exercise the short path, each tail block, the exactly-one-stripe boundary, and multi-stripe inputs; determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances; case sensitivity, single-character-change, and append-character avalanche guards; the full-character-distinction regressions shared with the other full-width string hashers (`StringFnV1AHasher` collides `"A"` / `"Ł"` and `"AB"` / `"Łł"`, `StringMetroHash64Hasher` keeps them distinct); a 1000-distinct-string distinctness sweep; `Hash(null)` throwing `ArgumentNullException`; and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<string, …, StringMetroHash64Hasher>` and `CeleritySet<string, StringMetroHash64Hasher>`, including the empty-string regular key, a non-ASCII key, the out-of-band `null` key, and a regression that `'A'` / `'Ł'` are kept as distinct dictionary keys on the probe path.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `StringMetroHash64Hasher` section (algorithm, the four 64-bit accumulators over 32-byte stripes, the post-loop lattice and tail handling, the xor-fold to 32 bits, the `metrohash64_1` constants, where it sits alongside `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` at the strong-avalanche top of the `string` ladder, the profile-to-choose framing against the xxHash family, the canonical-MetroHash64-over-UTF-16-folded equivalence and the `finalize(k2 * k0)` empty-string note) added after the `StringXxHash64Hasher` entry, the `StringFnV1AHasher` / `StringFnV1AFullHasher` / `StringFnV1A64Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` ladder lines updated so the strong tier consistently reads `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher`, the `StringXxHash64Hasher` XML docs re-pointed at it as a same-class peer, the choosing-a-hasher table's `string` row extended to list it, and the README `string` hasher-picking bullet extended with the MetroHash64 step. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` hasher spread now also constructs a `CelerityDictionary<string, int, StringMetroHash64Hasher>` so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiation.
- `StringCityHash64Hasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — a throughput-oriented strong-distribution hash provider for `string` keys using Google's CityHash64 algorithm (the `CityHash64` entry point of the v1.1 reference), over the string's native little-endian UTF-16 byte stream, xor-folded down to a signed 32-bit result. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. It joins `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` at the throughput-oriented strong tier called for in issue #24 (CityHash is explicitly on that issue's hash-function checklist). Unlike the single stripe loop of the xxHash / MetroHash family, CityHash64 is **length-classed**: the byte length selects one of four code paths — ≤ 16 bytes (`HashLen0to16`, a `ShiftMix` of two multiplied words or the single-/four-byte tail forms), 17–32 bytes (`HashLen17to32`, four words mixed through `HashLen16`), 33–64 bytes (`HashLen33to64`, eight words mixed with three `bswap_64` steps), and over 64 bytes the 56-byte-state main loop (two `WeakHashLen32WithSeeds` blocks plus `x`, `y`, `z`) consuming 64 bytes (thirty-two UTF-16 code units) per iteration after hashing the tail first — and all paths finalize through `HashLen16(u, v) = let a = (u^v)*kMul; a ^= a>>47; b = (v^a)*kMul; b ^= b>>47; b*kMul`, with the 64-bit result reduced to 32 bits by the standard xor-fold `h ^ (h >> 32)`. The dedicated short branches mean it spends less time in tail handling than a stripe-oriented hash on the short-to-mid keys typical of identifiers, so it often edges ahead there; `StringCityHash64Hasher`, `StringMetroHash64Hasher`, `StringXxHash64Hasher`, and `StringXxHash32Hasher` are peers at the throughput-oriented top of the ladder, to be chosen by profiling on the actual key shape. Like the other full-width string hashers it consumes the full 16-bit value of every character, so it distinguishes characters that differ only in their upper byte (e.g. `'A'` `U+0041` vs `'Ł'` `U+0141`, which `StringFnV1AHasher` collides on). `StringCityHash64Hasher.Hash(s)` equals canonical CityHash64 (v1.1) over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(s)` xor-folded to 32 bits; the empty string maps to the algorithm's length-`0` result — the constant `k2` (`0x9AE16A3B2F90404F`) — folded to 32 bits (its UTF-16 byte stream is zero bytes), and the dictionaries route the out-of-band `null` key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. `Hash(null)` throws `ArgumentNullException` (parameter `"key"`), matching the other string hashers. The mixing constants are the public-domain CityHash v1.1 values (`k0 = 0xC3A5C85C97CB3127`, `k1 = 0xB492B66FBE98F273`, `k2 = 0x9AE16A3B2F90404F`, `kMul = 0x9DDFEA08EB382D69`). `StringMetroHash64Hasher`'s neighbouring docs now cross-reference it as a same-class peer. Incremental progress on issue #24.
- `StringCityHash64HasherTests` — mirrors `StringMetroHash64HasherTests` for the CityHash64 path: a spec anchor asserting `Hash("")` equals the length-`0` result (the constant `k2`) folded to 32 bits (expressed symbolically from the constant so it pins the value and the fold direction without a hand-computed magic number, plus a companion test pinning the test's own byte-oriented reference's raw 64-bit empty-input result against the same `k2`); a twenty-eight-case spec-equivalence theory asserting the hasher equals a canonical, independently-written byte-oriented CityHash64 (v1.1) over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(...)` folded to 32 bits (the reference reads words with `BinaryPrimitives.ReadUInt64/UInt32LittleEndian` against raw byte offsets, so it shares no code with the hasher's char-loop and pins the four length-class dispatch boundaries, the end-relative offsets, the 64-byte main loop, byte order, the finalization, and the final fold) across ASCII / mixed / CJK / embedded-NUL / surrogate-pair inputs and lengths that exercise each of the four length classes plus the single-chunk and multi-chunk main loop; determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances; case sensitivity, single-character-change, and append-character avalanche guards; the full-character-distinction regressions shared with the other full-width string hashers (`StringFnV1AHasher` collides `"A"` / `"Ł"` and `"AB"` / `"Łł"`, `StringCityHash64Hasher` keeps them distinct); a 1000-distinct-string distinctness sweep; `Hash(null)` throwing `ArgumentNullException`; and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<string, …, StringCityHash64Hasher>` and `CeleritySet<string, StringCityHash64Hasher>`, including the empty-string regular key, a non-ASCII key, the out-of-band `null` key, and a regression that `'A'` / `'Ł'` are kept as distinct dictionary keys on the probe path.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `StringCityHash64Hasher` section (algorithm, the four length-classed code paths and the 64-byte main loop, the `HashLen16` finalizer, the xor-fold to 32 bits, the CityHash v1.1 constants, where it sits alongside `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` at the strong-avalanche top of the `string` ladder, the length-classed-edges-ahead-on-short-keys framing, the canonical-CityHash64-over-UTF-16-folded equivalence and the `k2` empty-string note) added after the `StringMetroHash64Hasher` entry, the `StringMetroHash64Hasher` ladder lines (XML docs and the hashing.md section) updated so the strong tier consistently reads `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher`, the choosing-a-hasher table's `string` row extended to list it, and the README `string` hasher-picking bullet extended with the CityHash64 step. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` hasher spread now also constructs a `CelerityDictionary<string, int, StringCityHash64Hasher>` so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiation.
- `StringSipHash24Hasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — a keyed, hash-flooding-resistant hash provider for `string` keys using the SipHash-2-4 pseudorandom function (Jean-Philippe Aumasson & Daniel J. Bernstein), over the string's native little-endian UTF-16 byte stream, xor-folded down to a signed 32-bit result. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. It is the first **keyed** entry on the `string` ladder and sits one rung beyond the throughput-oriented `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher` peers — SipHash is the hash-flooding-resistant PRF that Python, Ruby, and Rust's `HashMap` use by default for exactly this reason: an adversary who controls the keys cannot construct a flood of inputs that collide into one bucket and degrade the table to O(n) probe chains without recovering the secret key. It is an add-rotate-xor (ARX) construction over four 64-bit state words `v0..v3`: each 8-byte (four-UTF-16-code-unit) little-endian message word `m` is folded as `v3 ^= m`, two `SipRound`s, `v0 ^= m`; the final block packs the leftover 0–6 tail bytes into the low bytes and the input length (mod 256) into the top byte through the same step; finalization is `v2 ^= 0xFF` then four `SipRound`s, and the result `v0 ^ v1 ^ v2 ^ v3` is reduced to 32 bits by the standard xor-fold `h ^ (h >> 32)`. `SipRound` is the fixed add / rotate / xor lattice with left-rotation constants 13, 32, 16, 21, 17, 32, so the function carries no large table and stays branch-light, though it is slower than the multiply-and-rotate throughput family. Because Celerity hashers are zero-state structs that collections build via `new THasher()`, this hasher carries a **fixed** built-in key (the canonical SipHash reference key, bytes `00..0f`, read as `k0 = 0x0706050403020100`, `k1 = 0x0F0E0D0C0B0A0908`) rather than a per-process secret — enough for SipHash's strong avalanche and to defeat collision sets crafted against a weaker published algorithm, but not a turnkey secret-keyed defense (callers who need that should seed a per-process key out of band; the type documents the upgrade path). Like the other full-width string hashers it consumes the full 16-bit value of every character, so it distinguishes characters that differ only in their upper byte (e.g. `'A'` `U+0041` vs `'Ł'` `U+0141`, which `StringFnV1AHasher` collides on). `StringSipHash24Hasher.Hash(s)` equals canonical SipHash-2-4 (with the fixed key above) over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(s)` xor-folded to 32 bits; the empty string maps to the published length-`0` reference vector `0x726FDB47DD0E0E31` folded to 32 bits (its UTF-16 byte stream is zero bytes), and the dictionaries route the out-of-band `null` key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. `Hash(null)` throws `ArgumentNullException` (parameter `"key"`), matching the other string hashers. `StringCityHash64Hasher`'s neighbouring docs now cross-reference it as the keyed step beyond the throughput tier. Incremental progress on issue #24 (SipHash is explicitly on that issue's hash-function checklist).
- `StringSipHash24HasherTests` — mirrors `StringCityHash64HasherTests` for the SipHash-2-4 path: a spec anchor theory pinning the test's own byte-oriented reference against the **published official SipHash-2-4 test vectors** for the standard key (`00..0f`) over the canonical input `bytes[i] = i` at lengths 0 (`0x726FDB47DD0E0E31`), 1 (`0x74F839C593DC67FD`), and 15 (`0xA129CA6149BE45E5`), covering the length-0 path, a single tail byte, and a full compression word plus a 7-byte tail — so the reference is anchored to the spec independently of the hasher's char-loop; a `Hash("")` anchor asserting the empty string folds the length-`0` vector to 32 bits; a twenty-four-case spec-equivalence theory asserting the hasher equals a canonical, independently-written byte-oriented SipHash-2-4 over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(...)` folded to 32 bits (the reference reads message words with `BinaryPrimitives.ReadUInt64LittleEndian` against raw byte offsets and packs the tail / length byte from raw bytes, so it shares no code with the hasher's char-loop and pins the word order, the 0–3-char tail boundaries, the length byte, the two-round compression and four-round finalization, byte order, and the final fold) across ASCII / mixed / CJK / embedded-NUL / surrogate-pair inputs and lengths that exercise the tail-only path, the exactly-one-word and one-word-plus-each-tail boundaries, and multi-word inputs; determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances; case sensitivity, single-character-change, and append-character avalanche guards; the full-character-distinction regressions shared with the other full-width string hashers (`StringFnV1AHasher` collides `"A"` / `"Ł"` and `"AB"` / `"Łł"`, `StringSipHash24Hasher` keeps them distinct); a 1000-distinct-string distinctness sweep; `Hash(null)` throwing `ArgumentNullException`; and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<string, …, StringSipHash24Hasher>` and `CeleritySet<string, StringSipHash24Hasher>`, including the empty-string regular key, a non-ASCII key, the out-of-band `null` key, and a regression that `'A'` / `'Ł'` are kept as distinct dictionary keys on the probe path.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `StringSipHash24Hasher` section (algorithm, the ARX `SipRound` lattice and its rotation constants, the two-round compression / four-round finalization, the tail-and-length final block, the xor-fold to 32 bits, the fixed reference key and the keyed-but-fixed-key caveat with its upgrade path, where it sits one rung beyond the throughput-oriented peers as the keyed hash-flooding-resistant option, and the canonical-SipHash-2-4-over-UTF-16-folded equivalence with the `0x726FDB47DD0E0E31` empty-string note) added after the `StringCityHash64Hasher` entry, a forward pointer from the `StringCityHash64Hasher` **Note** to it, the choosing-a-hasher table's `string` row extended to list it as the untrusted-keys option, and the README `string` hasher-picking bullet extended with the SipHash step. `StringCityHash64Hasher`'s XML docs now cross-reference it as the keyed step beyond the throughput tier. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` hasher spread now also constructs a `CelerityDictionary<string, int, StringSipHash24Hasher>` so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiation.
- `StringHighwayHash64Hasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — a keyed, hash-flooding-resistant hash provider for `string` keys using Google's HighwayHash (64-bit output, designed by Jyrki Alakuijala, Bill Cox & Jan Wassenberg), over the string's native little-endian UTF-16 byte stream, xor-folded down to a signed 32-bit result. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. It is the **second keyed** entry on the `string` ladder and a peer to `StringSipHash24Hasher` at the keyed top, beyond the throughput-oriented `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher` peers — Google designed HighwayHash as a faster, SIMD-friendly successor to SipHash for the same hash-flooding-resistance job: an adversary who controls the keys cannot construct a flood of inputs that collide into one bucket without recovering the secret key. Where SipHash is a compact ARX lattice, HighwayHash keeps a wide four-lane state (`v0`, `v1`, `mul0`, `mul1`, four 64-bit words each) advanced 32 bytes (sixteen UTF-16 code units) at a time: each 32-byte packet runs the per-lane multiply-accumulate (`v1 += mul0 + lane`, `mul0 ^= (v1 & 0xffffffff) * (v0 >> 32)`, `v0 += mul1`, `mul1 ^= (v0 & 0xffffffff) * (v1 >> 32)`) then four `ZipperMergeAndAdd` byte-shuffle steps; a trailing partial packet (1–30 bytes here, since the UTF-16 stream is always even) mixes its length into `v0`, rotates `v1` by that length, and packs the leftover bytes into a zero-filled 32-byte block under the reference's alignment rules; finalization runs four permute-and-update rounds (reorder the `v0` lanes and swap their 32-bit halves, re-run the update) and returns `v0[0] + v1[0] + mul0[0] + mul1[0]`, reduced to 32 bits by the standard xor-fold `h ^ (h >> 32)`. That wide-state structure is built to vectorize onto AVX2 / NEON, which is where HighwayHash earns its throughput edge over SipHash; the current Celerity implementation is a **portable scalar** one — it produces the exact HighwayHash64 output (verified against the published reference test vectors) but explicit SIMD acceleration is a follow-up, so on a scalar code path it should not be assumed to out-run the unkeyed throughput family. Because Celerity hashers are zero-state structs that collections build via `new THasher()`, it carries a **fixed** built-in 256-bit key (the canonical HighwayHash reference key, bytes `00..1f` read as four little-endian 64-bit words) rather than a per-process secret — enough to defeat collision sets crafted against a weaker published algorithm, but not a turnkey secret-keyed defense (callers who need that should seed a per-process key out of band; the type documents the upgrade path), exactly mirroring the `StringSipHash24Hasher` caveat. Like the other full-width string hashers it consumes the full 16-bit value of every character, so it distinguishes characters that differ only in their upper byte (e.g. `'A'` `U+0041` vs `'Ł'` `U+0141`, which `StringFnV1AHasher` collides on). `StringHighwayHash64Hasher.Hash(s)` equals canonical HighwayHash64 (with the fixed key above) over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(s)` xor-folded to 32 bits; the empty string maps to the published length-`0` reference vector `0x907A56DE22C26E53` folded to 32 bits (its UTF-16 byte stream is zero bytes), and the dictionaries route the out-of-band `null` key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. `Hash(null)` throws `ArgumentNullException` (parameter `"key"`), matching the other string hashers. The state is held in stack-allocated `Span<ulong>` lanes (no heap allocation on the hot path). `StringSipHash24Hasher`'s neighbouring docs now cross-reference it as the SIMD-oriented second keyed option. Incremental progress on issue #24 (HighwayHash is explicitly on that issue's hash-function checklist).
- `StringHighwayHash64HasherTests` — mirrors `StringSipHash24HasherTests` for the HighwayHash64 path: a spec anchor asserting the test's own byte-oriented reference reproduces **every one of the 65 published official HighwayHash64 test vectors** for the reference key (bytes `00..1f`) over the canonical input `bytes[i] = i` at lengths 0–64 — independently of the hasher's char-loop — which together cover the length-0 path, the partial-packet remainder under each alignment (the `size_mod32 & 16` branch and the short `size_mod4` branch), the exact 32-byte packet boundary, and multi-packet inputs; a `Hash("")` anchor asserting the empty string folds the length-`0` vector `0x907A56DE22C26E53` to 32 bits; a twenty-six-case spec-equivalence theory asserting the hasher equals a canonical, independently-written byte-oriented HighwayHash64 over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(...)` folded to 32 bits (the reference reads 64-bit lanes with `BinaryPrimitives.ReadUInt64LittleEndian` against raw byte offsets and builds the remainder packet from raw bytes, so it shares no code with the hasher's char-loop and pins the lane order, the per-lane mix, the zipper-merge masks, the remainder alignment rules, the `Rotate32By` step, the four-round permute finalization, byte order, and the final fold) across ASCII / mixed / CJK / embedded-NUL / surrogate-pair inputs and lengths that exercise the remainder-only path, each remainder alignment, the exactly-one-packet boundary (16 chars), one-packet-plus-remainder, and multi-packet inputs; determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances; case sensitivity, single-character-change, and append-character avalanche guards; the full-character-distinction regressions shared with the other full-width string hashers (`StringFnV1AHasher` collides `"A"` / `"Ł"` and `"AB"` / `"Łł"`, `StringHighwayHash64Hasher` keeps them distinct); a 1000-distinct-string distinctness sweep; `Hash(null)` throwing `ArgumentNullException`; and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<string, …, StringHighwayHash64Hasher>` and `CeleritySet<string, StringHighwayHash64Hasher>`, including the empty-string regular key, a non-ASCII key, the out-of-band `null` key, and a regression that `'A'` / `'Ł'` are kept as distinct dictionary keys on the probe path.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `StringHighwayHash64Hasher` section (algorithm, the four-lane wide state, the per-lane multiply-accumulate and zipper-merge, the partial-packet remainder rules, the permute finalization, the xor-fold to 32 bits, the fixed reference key and the keyed-but-fixed-key caveat with its upgrade path, the portable-scalar-today / SIMD-tomorrow framing, where it sits as the second keyed peer to `StringSipHash24Hasher` at the top of the ladder, and the canonical-HighwayHash64-over-UTF-16-folded equivalence with the `0x907A56DE22C26E53` empty-string note) added after the `StringSipHash24Hasher` entry, a forward pointer from the `StringSipHash24Hasher` **Note** to it, the choosing-a-hasher table's `string` row extended to list it alongside `StringSipHash24Hasher` as the untrusted-keys options, and the README `string` hasher-picking bullet extended to present the two keyed options. `StringSipHash24Hasher`'s docs now cross-reference it as the SIMD-oriented second keyed option. - `StringSipHash13Hasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — a keyed, hash-flooding-resistant hash provider for `string` keys using the SipHash-1-3 pseudorandom function (Jean-Philippe Aumasson & Daniel J. Bernstein), over the string's native little-endian UTF-16 byte stream, xor-folded down to a signed 32-bit result. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. It is the **reduced-round** sibling of `StringSipHash24Hasher` and a third keyed peer (alongside `StringSipHash24Hasher` and `StringHighwayHash64Hasher`) at the keyed top of the `string` ladder, beyond the throughput-oriented `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher` peers. The two-digit suffix is the round count: SipHash-1-3 runs **one** `SipRound` of compression per 8-byte (four-char) message word and **three** `SipRound`s of finalization, versus two and four for SipHash-2-4 — halving the compression work makes it materially faster on every message word while keeping the keyed, well-distributed avalanche that defeats adversarial collision sets. This is exactly the construction Rust's standard-library `HashMap` uses by default (Python and Ruby use the conservative SipHash-2-4), chosen there as the sweet spot between throughput and flooding resistance. The `SipRound` lattice and its left-rotation constants (13, 32, 16, 21, 17, 32) are identical to `StringSipHash24Hasher`; only the round counts differ. Like the other full-width string hashers it consumes the full 16-bit value of every character, so it distinguishes characters that differ only in their upper byte (e.g. `'A'` `U+0041` vs `'Ł'` `U+0141`, which `StringFnV1AHasher` collides on). Because Celerity hashers are zero-state structs that collections build via `new THasher()`, it carries the same **fixed** built-in key as `StringSipHash24Hasher` (the canonical SipHash reference key, bytes `00..0f`, read as `k0 = 0x0706050403020100`, `k1 = 0x0F0E0D0C0B0A0908`) rather than a per-process secret — enough to defeat collision sets crafted against a weaker published algorithm, but not a turnkey secret-keyed defense (callers who need that should seed a per-process key out of band; the type documents the upgrade path). `StringSipHash13Hasher.Hash(s)` equals canonical SipHash-1-3 (with the fixed key above) over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(s)` xor-folded to 32 bits; the empty string maps to the length-`0` reference vector `0xABAC0158050FC4DC` folded to 32 bits (its UTF-16 byte stream is zero bytes), and the dictionaries route the out-of-band `null` key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. `Hash(null)` throws `ArgumentNullException` (parameter `"key"`), matching the other string hashers. `StringSipHash24Hasher`'s neighbouring docs now cross-reference it as the faster reduced-round variant. Prefer it over `StringSipHash24Hasher` when you want the keyed hash-flooding defense at the lowest cost; prefer `StringSipHash24Hasher` for the extra cryptographic margin of the conservative round counts. Incremental progress on issue #24 (SipHash is explicitly on that issue's hash-function checklist).
- `StringSipHash13HasherTests` — mirrors `StringSipHash24HasherTests` for the SipHash-1-3 path with a **single parameterized** byte-oriented reference (compression / finalization round counts as arguments) so the same machinery anchors both variants: a spec anchor running the reference at `(2, 4)` against the **published official SipHash-2-4 test vectors** for the standard key (`00..0f`) over the canonical input `bytes[i] = i` at lengths 0 (`0x726FDB47DD0E0E31`), 1 (`0x74F839C593DC67FD`), and 15 (`0xA129CA6149BE45E5`) — which pins the word order, tail packing, length byte, round structure, and rotation constants independently of the hasher, leaving the round counts as the only `(1, 3)`-specific change — plus a companion theory pinning the reference at `(1, 3)` against the SipHash-1-3 vectors for the same key / canonical input at lengths 0 (the published `0xABAC0158050FC4DC`), 1 (`0xC9F49BF37D57CA93`), and 15 (`0xD320D86D2A519956`); a `Hash("")` anchor asserting the empty string folds the length-`0` vector to 32 bits; a twenty-four-case spec-equivalence theory asserting the hasher equals the `(1, 3)` reference over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(...)` folded to 32 bits across ASCII / mixed / CJK / embedded-NUL / surrogate-pair inputs and lengths that exercise the tail-only path, the exactly-one-word and one-word-plus-each-tail boundaries, and multi-word inputs; a variant-distinctness guard asserting the SipHash-1-3 hash differs from `StringSipHash24Hasher` on a multi-word input (so the reduced round counts are provably wired through); determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances; case sensitivity, single-character-change, and append-character avalanche guards; the full-character-distinction regressions shared with the other full-width string hashers (`StringFnV1AHasher` collides `"A"` / `"Ł"` and `"AB"` / `"Łł"`, `StringSipHash13Hasher` keeps them distinct); a 1000-distinct-string distinctness sweep; `Hash(null)` throwing `ArgumentNullException`; and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<string, …, StringSipHash13Hasher>` and `CeleritySet<string, StringSipHash13Hasher>`, including the empty-string regular key, a non-ASCII key, the out-of-band `null` key, and a regression that `'A'` / `'Ł'` are kept as distinct dictionary keys on the probe path.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `StringSipHash13Hasher` section (algorithm, the one-compression / three-finalization round structure versus SipHash-2-4's two / four, the shared `SipRound` lattice and rotation constants, the Rust-`HashMap`-default framing, the fixed reference key and the keyed-but-fixed-key caveat with its upgrade path, where it sits as a third keyed peer to `StringSipHash24Hasher` / `StringHighwayHash64Hasher` at the top of the ladder, and the canonical-SipHash-1-3-over-UTF-16-folded equivalence with the `0xABAC0158050FC4DC` empty-string note) added before the `StringSipHash24Hasher` entry, a forward pointer from the `StringSipHash24Hasher` **Note** to it, the keyed-tier ladder lines across the `StringSipHash24Hasher` / `StringHighwayHash64Hasher` sections updated to read `StringSipHash13Hasher` / `StringSipHash24Hasher` / `StringHighwayHash64Hasher`, the `StringCityHash64Hasher` **Note**'s untrusted-keys pointer extended to the keyed family, the choosing-a-hasher table's `string` row extended to list it as the fastest keyed option, and the README `string` hasher-picking bullet extended to present the three keyed options. `StringSipHash24Hasher`'s XML docs now cross-reference it as the faster reduced-round variant. - `StringJenkinsOaatHasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — Bob Jenkins' **one-at-a-time** (OAAT) hash provider for `string` keys, over the string's native little-endian UTF-16 byte stream. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. It fills the cheap tier of the `string` escalation ladder between the FNV-1a variants and the strong block hashes: `StringFnV1AHasher` (cheapest, low-byte only) → `StringFnV1AFullHasher` / `StringFnV1A64Hasher` (cheap FNV-1a, full Unicode width) → `StringJenkinsOaatHasher` (cheap, full Unicode width, multiply-free, stronger per-bit avalanche than FNV-1a) → `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher` (strong avalanche, maximum throughput). For each input byte it runs the add / shift / xor lattice `hash += b; hash += hash << 10; hash ^= hash >> 6`, then finishes with the three-step avalanche `hash += hash << 3; hash ^= hash >> 11; hash += hash << 15`. Where FNV-1a folds each byte with a single xor and one multiply — and so has comparatively weak per-bit avalanche — the shift / xor steps give every input bit influence over the whole 32-bit result, yet the function uses **no multiplies at all** (only adds, shifts, and xors), keeping it in the same cheap cost class; it is the classic "better distributed than FNV-1a, cheaper than the block hashes" middle option. Like the other full-width string hashers it folds both bytes of every 16-bit code unit (low byte then high byte), so it distinguishes characters that differ only in their upper byte (e.g. `'A'` `U+0041` vs `'Ł'` `U+0141`, which `StringFnV1AHasher` collides on). `StringJenkinsOaatHasher.Hash(s)` equals the canonical one-at-a-time hash over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(s)`; the empty string maps to `0` (the accumulator starts at zero and the zero-preserving finalization steps leave it there), and the dictionaries route the out-of-band `null` key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. `Hash(null)` throws `ArgumentNullException` (parameter `"key"`), matching the other string hashers. Prefer it over the FNV-1a hashers when FNV-1a's single-multiply mixing clusters your keys but you do not want to pay for a block hash; escalate to the throughput-oriented strong family when the keys are clustered or adversarial enough to push long probe chains regardless. Incremental progress on issue #24.
- `StringJenkinsOaatHasherTests` — mirrors `StringFnV1AFullHasherTests` for the one-at-a-time path: a `Hash("")` anchor asserting the empty string maps to `0`; a twelve-case spec-equivalence theory asserting the hasher equals an independently-written byte-oriented one-at-a-time hash over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(...)` (a flat byte loop that shares no code with the hasher's char-loop, pinning the byte order and the per-byte mix) across ASCII / mixed / CJK / embedded-NUL / surrogate-pair inputs; an independent hand-computed value anchor for the single ASCII character `'a'` (running the OAAT steps over its two UTF-16 bytes `0x61` / `0x00`, sharing no code with either the hasher or the `Encoding.Unicode` reference); determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances; case sensitivity, single-character-change, and append-character avalanche guards; the full-character-distinction regressions shared with the other full-width string hashers (`StringFnV1AHasher` collides `"A"` / `"Ł"` and `"AB"` / `"Łł"`, `StringJenkinsOaatHasher` keeps them distinct); a 1000-distinct-string distinctness sweep; `Hash(null)` throwing `ArgumentNullException`; and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<string, …, StringJenkinsOaatHasher>` and `CeleritySet<string, StringJenkinsOaatHasher>`, including the empty-string regular key, a non-ASCII key, the out-of-band `null` key, and a regression that `'A'` / `'Ł'` are kept as distinct dictionary keys on the probe path.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `StringJenkinsOaatHasher` section (algorithm, the per-byte add / shift / xor lattice and the three-step avalanche, the multiply-free / stronger-than-FNV-1a-avalanche framing, where it sits in the cheap tier of the `string` ladder between the FNV-1a variants and the strong block hashes, and the canonical-OAAT-over-UTF-16 equivalence with the `0` empty-string note) added after the `StringFnV1A64Hasher` entry, the choosing-a-hasher table's `string` row extended to list it as the multiply-free cheap option with stronger per-bit avalanche than FNV-1a, and the README `string` hasher-picking bullet extended with the one-at-a-time step. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` hasher spread now also constructs a `CelerityDictionary<string, int, StringJenkinsOaatHasher>` so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiation.
- `StringXxHash3Hasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — the XXH3 algorithm (64-bit output, default 192-byte secret, seed `0`) for `string` keys, over the string's native little-endian UTF-16 byte stream, xor-folded down to a signed 32-bit result. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. This is the **third-generation** member of the xxHash family called for on issue #24's hash-function checklist (`XXHash family (XXH3, XXH32, XXH64)`), completing that family's three named entries alongside the existing `StringXxHash32Hasher` (XXH32) and `StringXxHash64Hasher` (XXH64), and the highest-throughput option in the box across most key shapes. Unlike the single stripe loop of XXH32 / XXH64, XXH3 mixes the input against a fixed secret and is **length-classed**: the 1–3, 4–8, and 9–16-byte classes XOR the relevant words against secret slices and run the `XXH64` / `rrmxmx` / `XXH3` avalanche; the 17–128 and 129–240-byte classes accumulate a sum of `XXH3_mix16B` 128-bit folds (`mul128_fold64(lo ^ secret, hi ^ secret)`); and inputs over 240 bytes run the eight-lane accumulator (`acc[lane ^ 1] += data; acc[lane] += (data ^ secret).lo32 * (data ^ secret).hi32`) over 64-byte stripes, scrambling (`xorshift 47`, `^ secret`, `* PRIME32_1`) once per 1024-byte block, then merge the lanes through four `mul128_fold64` rounds and a final `XXH3` avalanche. The dedicated short branches avoid the stripe-loop tail overhead that the single-loop hashers pay on the short-to-mid keys typical of identifiers, and the eight-lane bulk loop wins on long keys, so XXH3 typically leads on both ends of the length range; for very short keys `StringMurmur3Hasher`'s simpler loop has the least fixed overhead. The implementation is a **portable scalar** one — the conceptual UTF-16 byte stream is always even-length, so every read in the 4-to-240-byte and long paths lands on a char boundary and is built by combining whole `char`s; the single odd-aligned read XXH3 specifies (its 1-to-3-byte class) is reachable only for the byte length 2 (a one-char string) and is handled explicitly. The 192-byte secret is the canonical `XXH3_kSecret`, embedded as a non-allocating `ReadOnlySpan<byte>`; the eight accumulators live in a stack-allocated `Span<ulong>` (no heap allocation on the hot path). Like the other full-width string hashers it consumes the full 16-bit value of every character, so it distinguishes characters that differ only in their upper byte (e.g. `'A'` `U+0041` vs `'Ł'` `U+0141`, which `StringFnV1AHasher` collides on). `StringXxHash3Hasher.Hash(s)` equals canonical XXH3 64-bit (default secret, seed `0`) over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(s)` xor-folded to 32 bits; the empty string maps to the well-known canonical empty-input vector `0x2D06800538D394C2` folded to 32 bits, and the dictionaries route the out-of-band `null` key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. `Hash(null)` throws `ArgumentNullException` (parameter `"key"`), matching the other string hashers. `StringCityHash64Hasher`'s neighbouring docs now cross-reference it as the third-generation xxHash peer. `StringXxHash3Hasher`, `StringCityHash64Hasher`, `StringMetroHash64Hasher`, `StringXxHash64Hasher`, and `StringXxHash32Hasher` are peers at the throughput-oriented top of the `string` ladder, to be chosen by profiling on the actual key shape. Incremental progress on issue #24.
- `StringXxHash3HasherTests` — mirrors `StringXxHash64HasherTests` for the XXH3 path with an **independent, byte-oriented XXH3-64 reference** (reads the real `Encoding.Unicode` byte array with `BinaryPrimitives` at raw byte offsets and dispatches on byte length, sharing the XXH3 spec with the hasher but none of its char-loop reading / indexing code): an external anchor asserting the reference reproduces the **published canonical empty-input vector** `0x2D06800538D394C2` (pinning the reference's empty path, the secret bytes at offsets 56 / 64, and the `XXH64` avalanche to the spec independently of the hasher) plus a companion `Hash("")` anchor asserting the empty string folds that vector to 32 bits; a fifteen-case spec-equivalence theory asserting the hasher equals the reference over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(...)` folded to 32 bits across ASCII / mixed / CJK / embedded-NUL / single- and multi-surrogate-pair inputs spanning the 1-to-3, 4-to-8, 9-to-16, and 17-to-128-byte classes; a **length-boundary sweep** (`[MemberData]`) running the same hasher-vs-reference equivalence over 43 char lengths chosen to exercise every code-path edge — `0/1` (empty, 1-to-3), `2-4` (4-to-8), `5-8` (9-to-16), `9-64` (17-to-128), `65-120` (129-to-240), and `121+` up to `2049` chars including the `511/512/513`, `1023/1024/1025` boundaries that straddle the 1024-byte block loop so the multi-block accumulate / scramble / last-stripe path and the partial-block stripe count are all driven; determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances; case sensitivity, single-character-change, and append-character avalanche guards; the full-character-distinction regressions shared with the other full-width string hashers (`StringFnV1AHasher` collides `"A"` / `"Ł"` and `"AB"` / `"Łł"`, `StringXxHash3Hasher` keeps them distinct); a 1000-distinct-string distinctness sweep; `Hash(null)` throwing `ArgumentNullException`; and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<string, …, StringXxHash3Hasher>` and `CeleritySet<string, StringXxHash3Hasher>`, including the empty-string regular key, a non-ASCII key, the out-of-band `null` key, a 600-vs-601-char long-key pair that exercises the > 240-byte accumulator loop on the probe path, and a regression that `'A'` / `'Ł'` are kept as distinct dictionary keys.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `StringXxHash3Hasher` section (algorithm, the secret-driven length-classed dispatch, the eight-lane accumulator / scramble / merge of the long path, the xor-fold to 32 bits, where it sits alongside `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher` at the throughput-oriented strong-avalanche top of the `string` ladder, the leads-on-both-short-and-long-keys framing, and the canonical-XXH3-over-UTF-16-folded equivalence with the `0x2D06800538D394C2` empty-string vector) added after the `StringCityHash64Hasher` entry, the unkeyed-strong-tier ladder lines across the `StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher` / `StringSipHash13Hasher` / `StringSipHash24Hasher` / `StringHighwayHash64Hasher` sections updated so the throughput tier consistently reads `… StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher` / `StringXxHash3Hasher`, a forward pointer from the `StringCityHash64Hasher` **Note** to it, the choosing-a-hasher table's `string` row extended to list it as the typically-fastest length-classed-and-bulk option, and the README `string` hasher-picking bullet extended with the XXH3 step. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` hasher spread now also constructs a `CelerityDictionary<string, int, StringXxHash3Hasher>` so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiation.
- `StringHalfSipHash24Hasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — a keyed, hash-flooding-resistant hash provider for `string` keys using the HalfSipHash-2-4 pseudorandom function (Jean-Philippe Aumasson & Daniel J. Bernstein), over the string's native little-endian UTF-16 byte stream, producing a **native 32-bit** result. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. HalfSipHash is the **32-bit-word sibling** of `StringSipHash24Hasher`: the same keyed add-rotate-xor (ARX) construction and the same hash-flooding defence (an adversary who controls the keys cannot construct a flood of inputs that collide into one bucket without recovering the secret key), but built on four **32-bit** state words (`v0..v3`) advanced over 4-byte (two-char) message words instead of SipHash's 64-bit words over 8-byte words. That narrower state makes each round cheaper and the per-call fixed overhead lower, so on the short keys typical of identifiers — and on 32-bit-leaning targets — it is the faster keyed option, at the cost of the wider cryptographic margin the full 64-bit SipHash carries on long inputs. The `2-4` suffix is the round count, matching `StringSipHash24Hasher`: two `SipRound`s of compression per 4-byte (two-char) message word and four `SipRound`s of finalization, where `SipRound` here is the 32-bit add / rotate / xor lattice with left-rotation constants 5, 16, 8, 7, 13, 16. It joins `StringSipHash13Hasher` / `StringSipHash24Hasher` / `StringHighwayHash64Hasher` on the keyed tier of the `string` ladder, beyond the throughput-oriented `StringMurmur3Hasher` / `StringXxHash32Hasher` / `StringXxHash64Hasher` / `StringMetroHash64Hasher` / `StringCityHash64Hasher` / `StringXxHash3Hasher` peers, as the cheapest keyed choice for short keys. Unlike every other `string` hasher in the box, HalfSipHash's primitive output is **already 32 bits wide** (`v1 ^ v3` after finalization), so this hasher returns it directly — there is no 64-bit-to-32-bit xor-fold to lose entropy through. Like the other full-width string hashers it consumes the full 16-bit value of every character, so it distinguishes characters that differ only in their upper byte (e.g. `'A'` `U+0041` vs `'Ł'` `U+0141`, which `StringFnV1AHasher` collides on). Because Celerity hashers are zero-state structs that collections build via `new THasher()`, it carries a **fixed** built-in key (the canonical HalfSipHash reference key, bytes `00..07`, read as `k0 = 0x03020100`, `k1 = 0x07060504`) rather than a per-process secret — enough to defeat collision sets crafted against a weaker published algorithm, but not a turnkey secret-keyed defense (callers who need that should seed a per-process key out of band; the type documents the upgrade path), exactly mirroring the `StringSipHash24Hasher` caveat. The `v2`/`v3` initialization constants (`0x6C796765`, `0x74656462`) are the high 32 bits of SipHash's own `v2`/`v3` words; `v0`/`v1` start at zero. `StringHalfSipHash24Hasher.Hash(s)` equals canonical HalfSipHash-2-4 (4-byte output, with the fixed key above) over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(s)` reinterpreted as a signed 32-bit integer; the empty string maps to HalfSipHash-2-4's published length-`0` vector `0x5B9F35A9` (its UTF-16 byte stream is zero bytes, and there is no fold), and the dictionaries route the out-of-band `null` key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. `Hash(null)` throws `ArgumentNullException` (parameter `"key"`), matching the other string hashers. `StringSipHash24Hasher`'s neighbouring docs now cross-reference it as the cheaper 32-bit-word variant tuned for short keys. Prefer it over the 64-bit SipHash variants for short keys / 32-bit targets; escalate to `StringSipHash24Hasher` for the wider cryptographic margin on long inputs. Incremental progress on issue #24 (SipHash is explicitly on that issue's hash-function checklist).
- `StringHalfSipHash24HasherTests` — mirrors `StringSipHash24HasherTests` for the HalfSipHash-2-4 path with an independent, byte-oriented HalfSipHash-2-4 (4-byte output) reference (goes through `Encoding.Unicode` and reads 32-bit message words with `BinaryPrimitives.ReadUInt32LittleEndian` against raw byte offsets, handling a fully general 0–3-byte tail even though the even-length UTF-16 stream only yields a 0- or 2-byte tail, so it shares no code with the hasher's char-loop and pins the word order, tail packing, length byte, the two-round compression and four-round finalization, the 32-bit rotation constants, and the native 32-bit `v1 ^ v3` output): a spec anchor theory pinning that reference against the **published official `vectors_hsip32` HalfSipHash-2-4 vectors** for the standard key (`00..07`) over the canonical input `bytes[i] = i` at lengths 0 (`0x5B9F35A9`), 1 (`0xB85A4727`), 2 (`0x03A662FA`), 3 (`0x04E7FE8A`), 4 (`0x89466E2A`), 7 (`0xC563CF8B`), 8 (`0x8F84B8D0`), and 15 (`0x972BFE74`) — covering the length-0 path, each 1/2/3-byte sub-word tail, exactly one full 4-byte compression word, one word plus a 3-byte tail, two full words, and a multi-word input with a 3-byte tail — anchoring the reference to the spec independently of the hasher's char-loop; a `Hash("")` anchor asserting the empty string returns the length-`0` vector `0x5B9F35A9` directly (no fold); a twenty-three-case spec-equivalence theory asserting the hasher equals the reference over `Encoding.Unicode.GetBytes(...)` across ASCII / mixed / CJK / embedded-NUL / surrogate-pair inputs and lengths that exercise the tail-only path, the exactly-one-word and each one-word-plus-tail boundary, and multi-word inputs; determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances; case sensitivity, single-character-change, and append-character avalanche guards; the full-character-distinction regressions shared with the other full-width string hashers (`StringFnV1AHasher` collides `"A"` / `"Ł"` and `"AB"` / `"Łł"`, `StringHalfSipHash24Hasher` keeps them distinct); a 1000-distinct-string distinctness sweep; `Hash(null)` throwing `ArgumentNullException`; and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<string, …, StringHalfSipHash24Hasher>` and `CeleritySet<string, StringHalfSipHash24Hasher>`, including the empty-string regular key, a non-ASCII key, the out-of-band `null` key, and a regression that `'A'` / `'Ł'` are kept as distinct dictionary keys on the probe path.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `StringHalfSipHash24Hasher` section (algorithm, the 32-bit-word `SipRound` lattice and its rotation constants, the two-compression / four-finalization round structure, the native 32-bit `v1 ^ v3` output with no fold, the fixed reference key and the keyed-but-fixed-key caveat with its upgrade path, where it sits as the cheapest keyed option for short keys / 32-bit targets alongside `StringSipHash13Hasher` / `StringSipHash24Hasher` / `StringHighwayHash64Hasher`, and the canonical-HalfSipHash-2-4-over-UTF-16 equivalence with the published `0x5B9F35A9` empty-string vector) added after the `StringSipHash24Hasher` entry, a forward pointer from the `StringSipHash24Hasher` **Note** to it, the keyed-tier ladder lines across the `StringCityHash64Hasher` / `StringXxHash3Hasher` / `StringSipHash13Hasher` / `StringSipHash24Hasher` / `StringHighwayHash64Hasher` sections updated to read `StringHalfSipHash24Hasher` / `StringSipHash13Hasher` / `StringSipHash24Hasher` / `StringHighwayHash64Hasher`, the choosing-a-hasher table's `string` row extended to list it as the cheapest keyed option for short keys, and the README `string` hasher-picking bullet extended to present the four keyed options. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` hasher spread now also constructs a `CelerityDictionary<string, int, StringHalfSipHash24Hasher>` so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiation.
- `docs/performance.md`, `docs/migration.md`, `docs/troubleshooting.md`, and `docs/faq.md` — the four user-guide documents the "Improve documentation" roadmap item / issue [#15](https://github.com/marius-bughiu/Celerity/issues/15) was missing, alongside the already-shipped README usage examples, "choosing a collection" table, and API reference. **Performance tuning guide** covers the knobs in priority order: picking the type that matches the key, using the struct `foreach` / `Keys` / `Values` fast paths instead of the boxed `IReadOnlyDictionary<,>` / LINQ surface, pre-sizing `capacity` (power-of-two rounding, the `loadFactor` resize threshold, and `ICollection`-source auto-sizing), tuning `loadFactor` as a space-vs-time dial, the per-key-type hasher escalation ladders with an offline `HashQualityEvaluator` measurement step, freezing build-once `string` tables and verifying `IsPerfectlyHashed`, and running the BenchmarkDotNet suite locally in Release rather than trusting noisier CI numbers. **Migration guide** maps each BCL type (`Dictionary<int,V>` → `IntDictionary<V>`, `Dictionary<long,V>` → `LongDictionary<V>`, `Dictionary<K,V>` → `CelerityDictionary<K,V,THasher>`, `FrozenDictionary<string,V>` → `FrozenCelerityDictionary<V>`, `HashSet<T>` → the `*Set` types, `ILookup<,>` / `ToLookup` → `CelerityMultiMap<,,>`) and calls out the three differences that touch every migration — `IReadOnlyDictionary<TKey, TValue?>` instead of mutable `IDictionary<,>`, the nullable `TValue?` read surface, and unspecified iteration order — plus porting a custom `IEqualityComparer<TKey>` into a struct hasher, the `HashSet.Add` → `TryAdd` first-insertion-boolean swap, and a "what not to migrate" list. **Troubleshooting guide** lists the concrete compile errors (struct/`IHashProvider` constraint, missing third type argument, `IDictionary<,>` assignment, nullable warnings), runtime exceptions (`KeyNotFoundException` from the indexer getter, `ArgumentException` on duplicate `Add`, `InvalidOperationException` on mutate-during-`foreach`, the constructor `ArgumentOutOfRangeException` / `ArgumentNullException`), and behavioural surprises (the zero / `null` / `Guid.Empty` key working out-of-band, unspecified order, `IsPerfectlyHashed == false`, "no speedup vs `Dictionary<,>`" with the boxed-path / resize / clustering / wrong-type / Debug-build causes), each with a fix and a cross-link. **FAQ** answers the conceptual questions — thread safety, iteration order, the `TValue?` surface, default-key handling, tombstone-free backward-shift deletion, why hashers must be structs, hasher selection, `FrozenCelerityDictionary` vs `System.Collections.Frozen`, `CelerityMultiMap` vs `ToLookup`, AOT/trimming, target frameworks, and the package-split roadmap. The four guides are wired into the `docs/README.md` index (split into **Guides** and **API reference** sections) and the README's **Project docs** list, and the `ROADMAP.md` "Improve documentation" item is marked `done`. Documentation only — no production, test, or benchmark code changed.

### Tests

- `LongDictionary` cross-collection shared-test parity — `LongDictionary` was entirely absent from four shared test files that run the *same* checks across the dictionary family (`CelerityDictionary` / `IntDictionary`), even though it fully implements every surface they cover. This is the same `LongDictionary` ↔ family drift the CONTRIBUTING parity rule exists to prevent and that #136 closed for the dedicated enumeration suite; this closes it for the shared cross-collection suites (the step 4b parity facet). Pure test-coverage addition — no production code change. Files extended: (1) `ConstructorValidationTests.cs` — a `LongDictionary` block mirroring the `IntDictionary` loadFactor zero/negative and one-or-greater theories, negative-capacity, accept-zero-capacity, valid-loadFactor theory, plus the convenience-subclass-inherits-validation pair; (2) `LoadFactorBoundaryTests.cs` — `LongDictionary` low / high / tiny-capacity-multi-resize cases, the two out-of-band zero-key resize-timing cases, the parameterized across-load-factors theory, and a `long`-specific extreme-64-bit-key (`long.MinValue` / `long.MaxValue` / straddling-`int` / shared-low-32-bits) survive-resize tripwire for accidental 32-bit truncation; (3) `ReadOnlyDictionaryInterfaceTests.cs` — the full `IReadOnlyDictionary<long, TValue?>` surface (assignability, missing-key indexer throw, `TryGetValue` present / absent, `Keys` / `Values` including the zero key, generic + non-generic enumeration, mutation-throws-`InvalidOperationException`, LINQ `Count()` boxing path) plus an extreme-64-bit-key `Keys` round-trip, and the polymorphic-consumer helper extended to flow `LongDictionary` through an `IReadOnlyDictionary<long, long>` parameter alongside the existing two; (4) `IEnumerableConstructorTests.cs` — the `IEnumerable<KeyValuePair<long, TValue>>` ctor surface (null-source `ArgumentNullException` with `ParamName`, duplicate-key and duplicate-zero-key `ArgumentException`, still-validates-loadFactor, empty / array / list / deferred-non-collection sources, zero-key capture, large-source integrity, source-independence-after-construction, explicit-capacity-larger-than-count, the `LongDictionary<TValue, THasher>` generic base ctor, copy-from-another-`LongDictionary`, the `IReadOnlyDictionary` view) plus a `long`-specific extreme-key source-copy case; and (5) `AddAndTryAddTests.cs` — the previously-missing full `Add` / `TryAdd` mirror (`Add`: new key, duplicate, duplicate-leaves-value-unchanged, zero key, duplicate zero key, many keys, plus a `long`-specific extreme-key case; `TryAdd`: new key, false-on-existing, zero key, false-on-existing-zero, after-`Clear`) — the file previously had only the two `LongDictionary` indexer-overwrite regression cases. The full `Celerity.Tests` suite is green at 1269 tests on `net8.0` (65 new). Closes #138.
- `LongDictionaryEnumerationTests` — the dedicated enumeration suite that every other collection ships (`IntDictionaryEnumerationTests`, `CelerityDictionaryEnumerationTests`, `IntSetEnumerationTests`, `CeleritySetEnumerationTests`, `LongSetEnumerationTests`) but `LongDictionary` was missing, closing a `LongDictionary` ↔ `LongSet` test-coverage parity gap of exactly the kind the CONTRIBUTING parity rule exists to prevent. `LongDictionary<TValue, THasher>` already fully implements the enumeration surface (the version-checked struct `Enumerator` with `Reset`, the `KeyCollection` / `ValueCollection` views, and the boxed `IReadOnlyDictionary<long, TValue?>` paths), and `LongDictionaryTests` exercised a handful of enumeration cases inline, but the comprehensive cases the other collections pin were untested for `LongDictionary`. The new file (32 facts) mirrors `IntDictionaryEnumerationTests` for 64-bit keys and folds in the `long`-specific cases from `LongSetEnumerationTests`: empty / single / many-entry enumeration; the out-of-band zero key (included, yielded first, only-entry, and the zero-value projection through `Values`); `GetEnumerator` reflecting removal, zero-key removal, clear, and surviving repeated resizes from a tiny capacity; `MoveNext` / `Reset` throwing `InvalidOperationException` after a structural mutation (regular insert, zero-key insert, remove, zero-key remove, clear); the no-op-mutation guards that must *not* bump the version (duplicate `TryAdd`, absent `Remove`, clear-when-empty); `Keys` / `Values` distinctness and `Count`; the boxed `IEnumerable<long>` / `IEnumerable<int>` / `IEnumerable<KeyValuePair<long, long>>` and non-generic `IEnumerable` paths; `Reset` reusability and the post-exhaustion default `Current`; enumeration under a custom hasher (`Int64Murmur3Hasher`); LINQ over the dictionary; and a `long`-specific extreme-key sweep (`long.MaxValue` / `long.MinValue` / values straddling the `int` range / a lower-32-bits-share pair) that is a tripwire for accidental int-truncation on the enumeration path. Pure test-coverage addition — no production code change. Full `Celerity.Tests` suite is green at 1204 tests on `net8.0`. Closes #136.

### Changed

- All remaining `xUnit2013` warnings in `Celerity.Tests` are gone: `Assert.Equal(0, x.Count)` / `Assert.Equal(1, x.Count)` against a Celerity collection now use the xUnit-idiomatic `Assert.Empty(x)` / `Assert.Single(x)`, which produce better failure messages (`"The collection was expected to contain a single element, but it was empty"` instead of `"Expected: 1 / Actual: 0"`) and are also a strictly stronger assertion — `Assert.Single` enumerates and counts, so a collection whose `Count` property and enumerator disagree would now fail at this site instead of silently passing. The replacement is mechanical and spans 10 files: `RemoveOutValueTests.cs` (44 sites), `AddAndTryAddTests.cs` (28), `LongDictionaryTests.cs` / `IntDictionaryTests.cs` / `CelerityDictionaryTests.cs` / `CelerityDictionaryCollisionTests.cs` (8 each), `IEnumerableConstructorTests.cs` (6), `ReadOnlyDictionaryInterfaceTests.cs` (4), and `LongDictionaryCollisionTests.cs` / `IntDictionaryCollisionTests.cs` (2 each) — exactly the file list and call counts the weekly automated code-review sweep flagged. A clean `dotnet build --no-incremental` now produces zero `xUnit2013` warnings, so genuinely new warnings on a touched test file aren't drowned in 64 pre-existing ones. No test was added, removed, or weakened, and the full `Celerity.Tests` suite (818 tests) still passes locally on `net8.0`. Closes #123.

### Fixed

- Bulk `IEnumerable<…>`-source constructors now size the backing table to hold the whole source **without resizing**, honouring the documented "the source's `Count` is used to size the backing storage so inserts do not resize" contract that was previously not met (issue #27 — *Optimize resize operations* / *Reduce allocations in critical paths*). The resize threshold is `size × loadFactor`, so a table sized to the raw source count still tripped a full rehash-and-copy on the last inserts of the bulk fill (e.g. a 100-pair source at the default `0.75` load factor sized to `128`, threshold `96`, and rehashed into `256` near the end). The fix scales the requested capacity up by `1 / loadFactor` in each collection's `InitialCapacityForSource` helper, so a known-count source fits below the threshold and the bulk build is a single allocation with one hash per entry and zero rehashes. Applied uniformly to all seven hash-table collections — `IntDictionary`, `LongDictionary`, `CelerityDictionary`, `IntSet`, `LongSet`, `CeleritySet`, and `CelerityMultiMap` (distinct-key fills; duplicate-heavy sources simply leave slack, never resize). The plain `(capacity, loadFactor)` constructor is unchanged — `capacity` still rounds to the next power of two as documented — and `SmallDictionary` is exempt (no load factor, no hasher: its capacity-verbatim sizing already fills without resizing). The null-source-before-loadFactor-validation ordering (issue #94) is preserved: the new sizing math runs only after the `ArgumentNullException.ThrowIfNull(source)` check and is skipped for an out-of-range load factor, which the primary constructor still rejects with `ArgumentOutOfRangeException`. A non-collection source (unknown count) falls through to the plain capacity exactly as before.
- `BulkConstructorNoResizeTests` — a new cross-collection shared regression suite pinning the fix the same way `TryAddProbeCountTests` pins the single-probe contract: by counting `IHashProvider<T>.Hash` calls during construction. Building a table of `N = 100` distinct keys with no resize costs exactly `N` hash calls (one probe-chain walk per insert); a resize re-hashes every entry already placed, so a mid-build resize would push the count above `N`. Each of the seven hash-table collections gets a from-collection no-resize fact, plus a load-factor-scaling theory (`0.25` / `0.5` / `0.75` / `0.95`) asserting the headroom tracks a non-default load factor, and a non-collection-enumerable fallback case asserting correctness when the count is unknown. `SmallDictionary` is intentionally excluded (no hasher to count). The assertion fails on the pre-fix sizing and passes after it. Full `Celerity.Tests` suite is green at 1978 tests on `net8.0` (10 new).
- `MemoryAllocationBenchmark` extended with a `FromCollection` category — `Dictionary<int, int>`, `IntDictionary<int>`, and `CelerityDictionary<int, int, Int32WangNaiveHasher>` each built from a known-count `KeyValuePair<int, int>[]` source via the `IEnumerable` constructor, with the full `MemoryDiagnoser` columns. It sits alongside the existing `Grow` / `Presized` cases and makes the saved rehash-and-copy allocation visible. Part of the extended (weekly / on-demand) suite published to the `dev/bench-extended` dashboard; no `Program.cs` change needed (`MemoryAllocationBenchmark` is already registered).

## [1.4.0] - 2026-05-31

### Added

- `UInt32WangHasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — Thomas Wang's 32-bit integer hash (`hash32shift`) for `uint` keys, the `uint` counterpart to `Int32WangHasher`. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. It fills the missing middle tier of the `uint` escalation ladder: the `int` family ships `Int32WangNaiveHasher` (cheap XOR-fold default) → `Int32WangHasher` (full Thomas-Wang finalizer) → `Int32Murmur3Hasher` (strongest avalanche), and `long` mirrors it, but `uint` jumped straight from the cheap XOR-fold `UInt32Hasher` to `UInt32Murmur3Hasher` with no full-Wang option in between — `UInt32Hasher`'s own remarks pointed at escalating to the Murmur3 finalizer with nothing in the middle. The mixer is `~u + (u << 15)`, `^ (u >> 12)`, `+ (u << 2)`, `^ (u >> 4)`, `* 2057` (encoded as `+ (u << 3) + (u << 11)` to avoid the multiply), `^ (u >> 16)`, computed on the `uint` directly. A single shift-add-encoded multiply plus XOR-shift / shift-add rounds make it cheaper than the two-multiply `UInt32Murmur3Hasher` finalizer while still mixing every input bit. It is bijective on `uint`, so it is collision-free on any contiguous key range — only key structure, not the mixer, produces collisions. Because `Int32WangHasher` already runs the same `hash32shift` over the `uint` reinterpretation of its `int` key, `UInt32WangHasher.Hash(u)` returns exactly `Int32WangHasher.Hash((int)u)` for any matching 32-bit pattern. Unlike the Murmur3 finalizer, it does **not** map `0 → 0` (`Hash(0u) == -895235421`); the dictionaries route the out-of-band zero key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. Prefer it over the default `UInt32Hasher` when profiling shows the cheap XOR-fold is clustering; escalate to `UInt32Murmur3Hasher` for adversarial keys that need maximum avalanche. Incremental progress on issue #24.
- `UInt32WangHasherTests` — mirrors `Int32WangHasherTests` / `UInt32Murmur3HasherTests` for `uint` keys: seven exact-value anchors (`0u` / `1u` / `42u` / `1234567890u` / `int.MaxValue` / `int.MinValue` / `uint.MaxValue` bit patterns) whose expected values match the corresponding `Int32WangHasherTests` patterns and pin the round order / shift constants / `2057` multiply against drift, an eight-case theory asserting bit-for-bit equivalence with `Int32WangHasher` over the same 32-bit patterns, an explicit `Hash(0u) != 0` guard documenting the no-fixed-point property, determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances, a high-bit avalanche check, a 1000-consecutive-input collision-free sweep (exercising the bijective-on-`uint` property), a no-throw sweep over the key extremes, and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<uint, …, UInt32WangHasher>` and `CeleritySet<uint, UInt32WangHasher>` — including the out-of-band zero-key / zero-element slot and keys beyond `int.MaxValue`.
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `UInt32WangHasher` section (algorithm, where it sits on the cost-vs-avalanche curve relative to `UInt32Hasher` and `UInt32Murmur3Hasher`, the bit-pattern equivalence with `Int32WangHasher`, the no-`0 → 0` note) added between the existing `UInt32Hasher` and `UInt32Murmur3Hasher` entries, the choosing-a-hasher table's `uint` row updated to list `UInt32WangHasher` as the full-finalizer escalation option before `UInt32Murmur3Hasher`, and the README hasher-picking note updated to describe the two-step `uint` escalation (`UInt32WangHasher` → `UInt32Murmur3Hasher`) mirroring the `int` family. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` hasher spread now also constructs a `CelerityDictionary<uint, int, UInt32WangHasher>` so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiation.
- `UInt64WangHasher` in `Celerity.Hashing` — Thomas Wang's 64-bit integer hash (`hash64shift`) for `ulong` keys, the `ulong` counterpart to `Int64WangHasher`. Struct hasher, `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]`. Fills the missing alternative tier of the `ulong` family: until now `ulong` shipped a single hasher — `UInt64Hasher`, which is the Murmur3 `fmix64` finalizer (two 64-bit multiplies plus XOR-shifts) — while `int`, `long`, and (after #120) `uint` all offered a Wang full-finalizer option that costs less than the Murmur3 path while still mixing every input bit. The mixer is `~u + (u << 21)`, `^ (u >> 24)`, `+ (u << 3) + (u << 8)`, `^ (u >> 14)`, `+ (u << 2) + (u << 4)`, `^ (u >> 28)`, `+ (u << 31)`, computed on the `ulong` directly — only shifts and adds, no multiplies — so it is meaningfully cheaper per `Hash` call than the two-multiply `UInt64Hasher` while still giving every input bit influence over the result. It is bijective on `ulong`, so the only source of collisions is truncation to 32 bits when the result is returned — only key structure, not the mixer, produces collisions on the upper bits. Because `Int64WangHasher` already runs the same `hash64shift` over the `(ulong)key` reinterpretation of its `long` key, `UInt64WangHasher.Hash(u)` returns exactly `Int64WangHasher.Hash((long)u)` for any matching 64-bit pattern. Unlike the Murmur3 finalizer, it does **not** map `0 → 0` (`Hash(0UL) == -266614128`); the dictionaries route the out-of-band zero key around the hasher so this never collides with the empty-slot sentinel. Prefer it over the default `UInt64Hasher` when profiling shows the two 64-bit multiplies are a hot-path cost and the keys are already reasonably uniform; escalate back to `UInt64Hasher` for adversarial workloads that need maximum avalanche. Incremental progress on issue #24.
- `UInt64WangHasherTests` — mirrors `Int64WangHasherTests` / `UInt32WangHasherTests` for `ulong` keys: seven exact-value anchors (`0UL` / `1UL` / `42UL` / `1234567890123456789UL` / `long.MaxValue` / `long.MinValue` / `ulong.MaxValue` bit patterns) whose expected values match the corresponding `Int64WangHasherTests` patterns and pin the round order / shift constants / final `+ (u << 31)` step against drift, an eight-case theory asserting bit-for-bit equivalence with `Int64WangHasher` over the same 64-bit patterns (including non-`long` representable values like `0xDEADBEEFCAFEBABEUL` and `ulong.MaxValue`), an explicit `Hash(0UL) != 0` guard documenting the no-fixed-point property, determinism across calls and across stateless struct instances, a high-bit avalanche check (flipping bit 48), a 1000-consecutive-input collision-free sweep (exercising the bijective property), a no-throw sweep over the key extremes, and integration tests driving `CelerityDictionary<ulong, …, UInt64WangHasher>` and `CeleritySet<ulong, UInt64WangHasher>` — including the out-of-band zero-key / zero-element slot and `ulong.MaxValue` (beyond `long.MaxValue`).
- `docs/api/hashing.md` and `README.md` — a full `UInt64WangHasher` section (algorithm, where it sits on the cost-vs-avalanche curve relative to `UInt64Hasher`, the bit-pattern equivalence with `Int64WangHasher`, the no-`0 → 0` note) added immediately after the existing `UInt64Hasher` entry, the choosing-a-hasher table's `ulong` row updated from `default: UInt64Hasher` / `alternative: —` to list `UInt64WangHasher` as the lighter alternative when the two `fmix64` multiplies are a hot-path cost, and the README hasher-picking notes extended with a new `ulong` bullet describing the same drop-down (`UInt64Hasher` → `UInt64WangHasher`) and contrasting it with the `int` / `uint` family's two-step escalation up to the Murmur3 finalizer. The `Celerity.AotSmokeTest` hasher spread now also constructs a `CelerityDictionary<ulong, int, UInt64WangHasher>` so the Native AOT publish job compiles the new generic instantiation.

## [1.3.0] - 2026-05-24

### Changed

- The default hasher on the `LongDictionary<TValue>` and `LongSet` convenience subclasses switched from `Int64WangHasher` (the 11-op Thomas-Wang finalizer) to the new `Int64WangNaiveHasher` (a 2-op XOR-fold of `(int)key ^ (int)(key >>> 32) ^ (int)(key >>> 16)`). The previous default was paying roughly 5x the per-call hash cost of the naive variant on every `Hash`, and that cost compounds across the resize chain (every entry is re-hashed on resize) and across the backward-shift cluster scans (every scanned slot is re-hashed for the cyclic comparison) — the live benchmark dashboard was showing the `Long*` collections losing to BCL across every shape (1.62x on `LongDict.Insert(100k)`, 1.48x on `LongSet.Add(100k)`, 1.29-1.32x on `LongDict` / `LongSet.Remove`) almost entirely because of that. The middle-16-bits fold in the new default keeps enough high-half entropy alive that keys whose low 32 bits are sequential but upper bits carry structure (type tags, shard IDs, monotonically allocated IDs) still distribute reasonably — `Int64WangNaiveHasher` is intended as the new common-case fast path, not as a uniform replacement. **This is a user-visible behavioural change** on the parameterless `new LongDictionary<TValue>()` / `new LongSet()` constructors: callers who picked the convenience subclasses for adversarial-key collision behaviour will silently get an XOR-fold instead of the full Wang finalizer. Opt back into the previous default with the open-generic shape: `new LongDictionary<TValue, Int64WangHasher>()` / `new LongSet<Int64WangHasher>()`, or escalate further to `Int64Murmur3Hasher` for better avalanche under adversarial workloads. `Int64WangHasher` stays in the box and remains the right answer for those workloads; the choosing-a-hasher table in `docs/api/hashing.md` documents the new selection axis. Pinned by the new `Int64WangNaiveHasherTests` coverage (theory inputs across zero / `1` / `-1` / `42` / `long.MaxValue` / `long.MinValue` / `1234567890123456789L` / 48-bit-set / alternating-nibble keys, determinism across calls and struct instances, high-bit and mid-bit influence regression guards, consecutive-small-input and single-high-bit-strided distinctness sweeps) plus the existing 16-file `LongDictionary` / `LongSet` test surface running green against the new default. Closes #108.
- `BackwardShiftRemove` replaces `RehashAfterRemove` on `IntDictionary<TValue, THasher>`, `LongDictionary<TValue, THasher>`, `CelerityDictionary<TKey, TValue, THasher>`, `IntSet<THasher>`, `LongSet<THasher>`, and `CeleritySet<T, THasher>` — the new algorithm is Knuth TAOCP Vol 3, §6.4 Algorithm R. The previous `RehashAfterRemove` walked the cluster forward and, for every surviving entry, re-hashed the key and re-probed from its natural slot for an empty target; the inner probe was a second pass over the cluster, making the work-per-cluster quadratic where it should be linear. The dashboard was showing `Remove` losing to BCL across every collection, badly at small `N` (up to 5.10x on `CeleritySet_Remove(1k)` and 2.46x on `IntSet_Remove(1k)`) because of this. Backward-shift visits each surviving cluster entry exactly once and only moves it when the cyclic invariant says it must — for the gap at `i` and the scan position `j` with natural slot `k`: if `i <= j` bypass when `i < k && k <= j`, otherwise (`i > j`, the scan has wrapped) bypass when `i < k || k <= j`. The `Remove` call site no longer pre-clears the slot; the new helper writes the final empty entry itself, saving a redundant store on the common path where shifts collapse the gap. `Count`, the out-of-band zero / default key, `_version`-based enumerator invalidation, and the contiguous-cluster invariant under linear probing are all preserved. Pinned by one new wrap-around regression test per collection driven by a new `IdentityIntHasher` / `IdentityLongHasher` (cluster crossing the array boundary, mixed natural slots — exercises all three branches of the cyclic comparison), plus the existing `ResizeThenRemoveSweep_*_UnderFullCollision` facts continuing to pin the long-chain behaviour after multiple resizes.
- `[MethodImpl(MethodImplOptions.AggressiveInlining)]` is now applied to every `ProbeForInsert` / `ProbeForKey` / `ProbeForItem` / `BackwardShiftRemove` on all six collections (`IntDictionary`, `LongDictionary`, `CelerityDictionary`, `IntSet`, `LongSet`, `CeleritySet`). The hashers themselves already carried the attribute, but the methods that called them did not — every probe-driven entry point (`TryAdd`, the indexer get/set, `Remove`, `Contains`) crossed a function boundary on every operation even though the probe + shift methods are short and called from a small number of sites. With the hint, the JIT collapses the `probe → hash → mask → compare` pipeline into the caller's stack frame and folds the loop body into a tight `mov` + `and` + `cmp` + `je` sequence. Mechanical change — every behavioural and surface contract is unchanged.
- The probe and shift hot paths on all six collections now read `_keys[index]` / `_slots[index]` via `Unsafe.Add` against `MemoryMarshal.GetArrayDataReference` instead of the indexer, eliminating the per-iteration bounds check on every probe walk and every shift step. Sites updated: `ProbeForInsert` + `ProbeForKey` + `BackwardShiftRemove` + the indexer setter writes + the `TryAdd` writes on the dictionaries; `ProbeForItem` + the inline probe loop in `TryAdd` + `BackwardShiftRemove` + the `TryAdd` write on the sets. The safety invariant is structural: every collection has `mask = _keys.Length - 1` and `index = ... & mask`, so `index ∈ [0, _keys.Length)` is guaranteed for every iteration. Casts use the `(nint)(uint)` pattern so the JIT widens via zero-extension on x64 and gets the non-negativity hint that unlocks the tightest emit. The collision suites (every `ConstantIntHasher` / `ConstantLongHasher` fact plus the new `IdentityIntHasher` / `IdentityLongHasher` wrap-around facts) drive the probe loop right up against the cyclic boundary and would fault loudly on any off-by-one. Lookup-only paths (`ContainsKey`, `TryGetValue`, indexer getter, `Contains`) were already winning vs BCL and were left to a follow-up; that follow-up is tracked in PR #106.
- `ProbeForInsert` on `IntDictionary<TValue, THasher>`, `LongDictionary<TValue, THasher>`, and `CelerityDictionary<TKey, TValue, THasher>` now returns a `bool wasEmpty` out-parameter so the indexer setter and `TryAdd` no longer have to re-read `_keys[index]` after the probe to decide between bump-`_count` and overwrite. The probe loop already knows whether it exited on an empty slot or on a key match; lifting that signal to the caller saves one bounds-checked array load per insert (and one `EqualityComparer<TKey>.Default` dispatch on `CelerityDictionary`) on the hot insert path. The `_keys` hoist + single `mask` computation at the top of `ProbeForInsert` is now shared between the wrap step and the BCE arithmetic. The sets inline their probe loop in `TryAdd` already, so there is no separate `ProbeForInsert` to refactor on the set side. Pinned by `IntDictionary` / `LongDictionary` / `CelerityDictionary` `_Add_ThenIndexerOverwrite_ShouldSucceed` facts and the new `Dictionaries_RepeatedIndexerOverwrite_ShouldKeepCountStable` 100-round stress test across all three shapes (catches a wrong-branch wire-up in the `wasEmpty` signal that would only surface under aggregate workloads).
- The value-read paths on `IntDictionary<TValue, THasher>`, `LongDictionary<TValue, THasher>`, and `CelerityDictionary<TKey, TValue, THasher>` now read `_values[index]` via `Unsafe.Add` against `MemoryMarshal.GetArrayDataReference` instead of the indexer. This applies the same bounds-check-elimination pattern already used on the write paths (indexer setter, `TryAdd`, `Resize`, `BackwardShiftRemove`, `ProbeForInsert`, `ProbeForKey`) to the symmetric read sites: the `this[key]` getter's `return _values[index]!;`, `TryGetValue`'s `value = _values[index];`, `Remove(key, out TValue?)`'s `value = _values[index];` (which the void `Remove(key)` overload delegates through), and the per-iteration `keys[i]` / `values[i]` reads inside `ContainsValue`'s `O(n)` scan loop. The bounds are structural — `ProbeForKey` only returns `-1` (handled before the read) or a valid index into `_keys`, and `_keys.Length == _values.Length` is a class invariant preserved across construction / `Resize` — so the per-element BCE removal is provably safe. `ContainsValue` additionally hoists `keys.Length` into a local before the loop so the JIT sees a clean trip-count bound and folds the `keys` read into a sequential-add walk against the cached base reference. Casts use the `(nint)(uint)` pattern to give the JIT the non-negativity hint that unlocks a zero-extending load on x64. No public-API change; correctness is unchanged. Pinned by the existing read-heavy coverage (`*Tests`, `*CollisionTests` covering every read path under maximum probing pressure, `ContainsValueTests`, `RemoveOutValueTests`, `ReadOnlyDictionaryInterfaceTests`, `LoadFactorBoundaryTests`) — all 746 tests pass locally on `net8.0`.
- The struct `Enumerator.MoveNext()` on `IntDictionary<TValue, THasher>`, `LongDictionary<TValue, THasher>`, `CelerityDictionary<TKey, TValue, THasher>`, `IntSet<THasher>`, `LongSet<THasher>`, and `CeleritySet<T, THasher>` now walks the backing array via `Unsafe.Add` against `MemoryMarshal.GetArrayDataReference` instead of the indexer, applying the same bounds-check-elimination pattern already used on `ProbeForInsert` / `ProbeForKey` / `BackwardShiftRemove` / `Resize` and on the dictionary read paths (`this[key]` getter, `TryGetValue`, `Remove(out value)`, `ContainsValue`). The loop bound is unchanged — `++_index < length` keeps `_index ∈ [0, length)` — so the per-iteration `Buffer.GetMaximumArrayLength` / cmp-against-`Length` checks on the `keys[_index]` / `values[_index]` / `slots[_index]` reads drop out and the loop body is one compare + one indirect load + (on dictionaries) one paired indirect load instead of two-or-three bounds-checked reads. The dictionary enumerator also avoids the previous double-read of `keys[_index]` by caching the slot in a local before the empty-key comparison; the `CelerityDictionary` and `CeleritySet` variants additionally cache `EqualityComparer<TKey>.Default` / `EqualityComparer<T>.Default` outside the loop so the per-iteration comparer dispatch is hoisted out. The `Keys.Enumerator` / `Values.Enumerator` views on the three dictionaries forward `MoveNext` to the same entry struct enumerator, so they pick up the optimization for free.
